Abstract
The medical technology company BrainCapture aims at delivering EEG solutions to LMICs
to counterattack the treatment gap seen for people with undiagnosed epilepsy. They seek
to do this through their solution which is centered around a mobile application. However,
this application has never been assessed by an user experience expert, and through user
tests it was seen that the application has usability weaknesses and challenges.
This thesis focuses on discovering who the users of an EEG solution are and their cor-
responding user needs through expert interviews and EEG observations. Hereafter, the
BrainCapture solution will be assessed through a variety of user experience tools and
methodologies to adjudge whether the development of an optimized version of the solu-
tion is justifiable.
The analysis will show that this is justifiable, and therefore the design implementation of an
optimized version of the BrainCapture solution in terms of user interface and experience
at an MVP state will begin. The optimized version will then be refined iteratively through
the build-measure-learn cycle. This cycle will continue for five sprints, where usability
testing with relevant users will continuously improve the prototype.
Lastly, the findings and limitations of the thesis will be discussed and it will be determined
that the optimized version of the BrainCapture application is better in terms of usability
and based on user’s opinions.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX

Glossary
LMIC: Low and middle income countries
HIC: High income countries
AED: Anti epileptic drugs
EEG: Electroencephalogram (recording of brain activity)
ECG: Electrocardiogram (recording of heart rhythm)
EMG: Electromyography (recording of motor neuron activity)
EMU: Epilepsy monitoring unit
MVP: Minimum viable product
Sundhedsplatformen: Internal medical system

1
Introduction
This thesis is collaborative project between a MSc. Human-Centered Artificial Intelligence
student, Iman Chelhi, and BrainCapture Aps. The thesis has four main parts.
The first part focuses on discovering the user needs for EEG examinations. This is done
by accumulating different types of qualitative data such as expert interviews and obser-
vations.
The second part focuses on adjudging the user interface and user experience of the ex-
isting BrainCapture prototype through different methods, and the risks within the overall
solution are then analyzed to assess whether it is justified to build an optimized version
of the prototype.
The third part aims at building the optimized version of the prototype at a MVP level based
on the accumulated data and findings from the previous parts. Once the optimized version
has been through design implementation, it will go through multiple sprints where users
will test the prototype through usability tests, and then be improved iteratively based on
the results.
This will result in a prototype where user interface and user experience has been optimized
through a data-driven approach which is centered around the user.
Lastly, the final part ties in the thesis by discussing different aspects in terms of improve-
ments and limitations of the overall project.
1.1
Problem Background
Epilepsy is a neurological brain disease characterized by unprovoked seizures which can
negatively affect cognition [1]. The World Health Organization states that at least 50 mil-
lion people worldwide suffer from epilepsy, and of these 50 million, approximately 80%
are located outside HICs [2].
Epilepsy requires treatment and for this to be possible, a diagnoses is needed. How-
ever, this is a challenge in many LMICs, where lack of trained doctors and staff, relevant
equipment, available AED, and diagnostic facilities make it difficult to get diagnosed.
This is where BrainCapture wants to enter the market with their solution to help people
with epilepsy who go undiagnosed in LMICs.
1.2
State of the art
1.2.1
The typical clinical solution
The typical solution utilized in many hospitals, for conducting a standard 30 minute routine
EEG, includes EEG electrodes, either individual or in the form of a cap, conductive salt
2
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:10==
water based gel, an exfoliating NuPrep gel, and a syringe with a needle. Other electrodes
including ECG, EMG, eye eletrodes are also commonly used to create a multi-modal
examination.
The EEG electrodes, where the amount varies between 19-27 electrodes, are placed
on the patient’s head using the gels following a standardized 10-20 system. Within this
system, each electrode is placed at distances of 10 and 20%. Of the electrodes, two
of them differ as one is used as a reference (typically referred to as the REF electrode),
while the other is used as a ground (typically referred to as the GND electrode), and these
electrodes are placed before the rest.
For the rest of the electrodes, they are represented through letters and numbers. The
letters depict the position of the electrode above the brain lobe, and the numbers reference
a position for the electrode on either the left (odd numbers) or right (even numbers) side
of the brain.
The ECG electrodes, where around six electrodes, are typically placed on the rib cage,
and the eye electrode is placed on the eyelid. EMG electrodes are not a part of stan-
dard procedures, however they can be utilized if the patient is known to have myoclonic
movement. Then the EMG electrodes are placed on the corresponding muscles.
Once all the electrodes have been placed, they are connected to an amplifier and the
standard 30 minute routine EEG can be conducted. The data is then transferred as a file
to a computer. Hereafter, the file is uploaded to the cloud and other relevant roles can
then access the file to continue the diagnosis.
1.2.2
The BrainCapture solution
BrainCapture’s solution is a more compromised version of the typical clinical solution. The
BrainCapture solution offers a package including 27 EEG electrodes in caps of different
sizes, an amplifier, ECG device with two electrodes, and a mobile application.
While the EEG electrodes (cap) are placed the same as with the typical clinical solution,
the ECG is placed on the patient’s wrists. Furthermore, no eye or EMG electrodes are
included.
The included electrodes are then connected to the amplifier which is connected to the
application through Bluetooth. With the application, it is possible to conduct a standard
30 minute routine EEG.
The data is then converted into a file which is uploaded to the cloud, where, like the typical
clinical solution, relevant roles can access the file.
1.2.3
Other solutions
There are different solutions available for recording EEG. A competitor analysis conducted
in [1] gave an overview of three other major medical companies who deliver traditional
medical grade or wireless solutions focusing on number of channels, price, and mobility.

1.3
Problem Statement & Research Questions
BrainCapture is currently working on their application in regards to implementation and
testing. However, the user interface and user experience have not been evaluated by
experts within user experience.
This leaves questions in regards to whether the current application is the most optimal for
BrainCapture and their intended users.
Therefore, this thesis focuses on the following problem statement:
How might we adjudge the user interface & user experience of the BrainCapture
application to create an optimal version in terms of user interface & user experience
using data-driven approaches?
To answer this problem statement, the following research questions have been stated:
1. Who are the users an EEG solution and what are their needs?
2. What is the as-is state of the current user interface & user experience of the Brain-
Capture application?
3. How would an optimized version of the BrainCapture application look in terms of
user interface & user experience?
4
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:12==
2
Methodologies
Throughout the thesis, different methodologies and tools have been used to work system-
atically towards a final end result. The used methods for each chapter will be described
in theory and in practice for this thesis.
2.1
Research
2.1.1
Qualitative data collection: Expert interviews & observations
Qualitative data is characterized as descriptive data based on subjective opinions and
viewpoints, utilized to describe subjects, notions, inter alia. Within qualitative data collec-
tion, creating an understanding of the data subject’s experiences is the focal point. This
type of data typically does not include numerical values [3, 4].
Different tools can be used to accumulate qualitative data, and in this thesis, interviews
done on experts within EEG and epilepsy have been conducted (A.1). Here, the experts
of different roles have been questioned to gather an overview of user needs. Further-
more, standard EEG scans conducted at Sjællands Universitetshospital, Roskilde were
observed for the same purpose.
2.1.2
Affinity mapping
Affinity mapping, also sometimes referred to as affinity diagramming, is a tool used for
organizing or grouping data by recognizing common patterns, tendencies, or relationships
across through characteristics and themes [3]. This technique is beneficial for discovering
common issues or challenges and is therefore a valuable tool to use at the start of a project
during the research phase.
In this thesis, the affinity mapping tool was used to categorize main user needs through
the accumulated qualitative data.
2.2
Analysis
2.2.1
User experience review
User experience reviews are evaluations of a service or product conducted by an expert
within user experience focusing especially on usability challenges. Here, the expert thinks
of what could be a potential issue for the user. The expert can utilize different heuristics
as guidelines, but the existing knowledge of the expert is what distinguishes this tool from
other kinds of reviews [5].
For the thesis, the BrainCapture prototype was assessed through an user experience
review focusing on usability challenges. In order to capture a full overview of the prototype,
strengths were also included.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
5
==PAGE:13==
2.2.2
Peter Morville’s User Experience Honeycomb
The User Experience Honeycomb is a framework expanded from the users, content and
context diagram. The Honeycomb is focused on diving in to different aspects of user
experience. The aspects are categorized through seven sections which are centered
around whether the user finds a service or product usable, useful, desirable, findable,
accessible, and credible [6].
The usable section focuses on ease of use and familiarity, and the useful section is cen-
tered around user needs. The desirable section is about the visual user interface design,
the findable section targets easy navigation and access to information, and the acces-
sible section looks at how to accommodate users with disabilities. The credible section
examines the credibility of the subject, and lastly, the valuable section is an accumulation
of all the findings focusing on whether the subject brings value to the user.
While it is not mandatory for a product or service to equally satisfy all the sections, the
framework is useful when assessing good user experience.
In this thesis, the User Experience Honeycomb was used to assess the user interface
and experience of the BrainCapture prototype. This was done using a variety of tools to
adjudge the different sections within the Honeycomb. The tools for the usable, desirable,
accessible, and credible sections will be described below.
2.2.3
Jakob Nielsen’s 10 Usability Heuristics for User Interface Design
The 10 Usability Heuristics for User Interface Design are high level heuristics where each
heuristic focuses on different aspects within usability. Because of its simplicity, this method
can be used by different roles without a prior background in user experience resulting in
a rise of popularity for this method. The UX book describes the heuristics as a method
that novices can grab onto and use without a great deal of training [3].
As the name implies, there are 10 heuristics namely, Visibility of system status, Match
between system and the real world, User control and freedom, Consistency and stan-
dards, Error prevention, Recognition rather than recall, Flexibility and efficiency of use,
Aesthetic and minimalist design, Help users recognize, diagnose, and recover from errors,
and lastly, Help and documentation.
This method however has limitations within its simplicity as usability related challenges
can go undiscovered and can therefore not stand alone [3]. To accommodate this lim-
itation, an user experience inspection focusing on the heuristics will be utilized in this
thesis to adjudge the usable section in terms of usability strengths and weaknesses of
the BrainCapture prototype.
2.2.4
Schneiderman’s 8 Golden Design Rules
The 8 Golden Design Rules by Ben Schneiderman have been developed as a guideline for
designers to use for identifying challenges within user interface design which also affect
the user experience [7]. Like the aforementioned 10 Usability Heuristics, the 8 Golden
6
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:14==
Design Rules are a high level set of rules which are easy to comprehend for inexperienced
roles.
Schneiderman’s 8 Golden Design Rules are Strive for consistency, Enable frequent users
to use shortcuts, Offer informative feedback, Design dialog to yield closure, Offer simple
error handling, Permit easy reversal of actions, Support internal locus of control, and lastly,
Reduce short-term memory load.
In this thesis, the 8 Golden Design Rules have been used to assess whether the Brain-
Capture prototype fulfills the desirable section of the Honeycomb.
2.2.5
7 Principles of Universal Design
The 7 Principles of Universal Design is a set of principles commonly used by designers to
cover the broad term of accessibility which focuses on accommodating users with diverse
disabilities [8].
The 7 Principles of Universal Design are Equitable use, Flexibility in use, Simple and
intuitive use, Perceptible information, Tolerance for error, Low physical effort, and lastly,
Size and Space for Approach and Use.
The principles can be used for both designing a product or service from scratch, or to
assess an existing one, and in this thesis, the 7 Principles of Universal Design have been
applied on the BrainCapture prototype to adjudge whether it fulfills the accessible section
of the Honeycomb.
2.2.6
The Elements of Value Pyramid
The Elements of Value Pyramid is a hierarchical framework consisting of 30 elements
which are sectioned in four levels, namely Functional, Emotional, Life changing, and So-
cial impact. The framework was developed in order to make it easier to evaluate value of
a product or service from a user’s perspective.
Some elements are defined as having an inwardly focus as they are targeting user needs,
while other elements are described as having an outwardly focus as they look at holistic
aspects for the user [9].
Again, it is not mandatory for a product or service to satisfy all 30 elements, and at times
it is not a necessity, but targeting some elements across the four levels can be beneficial
when wanting to create value for the user and therefore manifesting credibility.
The Elements of Value Pyramid method has in this thesis been used for assessing whether
the BrainCapture prototype satisfies the credible section of the Honeycomb.
2.3
Design Implementation & Testing
2.3.1
MoSCoW Prioritization
The MoSCoW Prioritization method was developed as a tool to help prioritize tasks or
functionalities by filtering them as must have, should have, could have, and will not have.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
7
==PAGE:15==
The must have cover functionalities which are necessary and therefore can not be ex-
cluded, and while the should have are immensely valuable, they can be excluded. Could
have focus on functionalities which are desirable but optional as they bring less value,
and lastly, will not have defines functionalities which are shallow and bring minimal value
[10].
Through this filtering, a team can determine which functionalities or tasks to focus on and
include in their product or service.
In this thesis, the MoSCoW Prioritization is utilized to filter which functionalities that are
to be included in the final end product of the project.
2.3.2
Lean thinking: Build-Measure-Learn cycle
Lean thinking focuses on creating user value by using minimum resources through con-
tinuous testing [11].
Within Lean thinking is the Build-Measure-Learn cycle which is made of three phases
that move iteratively. Within the Build phase, a product or service is developed in its
lowest form meaning at a minimum viable product (MVP) state. Hereafter, the MVP is
validated through relevant testing methods in the Measure phase. In the Learn phase, the
accumulated data from the previous phase is evaluated to discover findings and insights
for possible improvements. As the cycle is iterative, it can continue until a satisfactory
end state has been reached [11].
The development of the optimized application follows the Build-Measure-Learn cycle,
where each version of the prototype is built whereafter they are measured through usabil-
ity testing, and lastly, the improvements are discovered based on the test results through
the Learn phase.
2.3.3
High fidelity (Hi-Fi) Prototyping
A high fidelity prototype is a detailed depiction of the user interface and interaction of a
service or product. It allows for users to fully comprehend the whole solution and can
gather more in depth results [3].
As mentioned, Lean thinking focuses on using minimum resources which also points to-
wards developing low fidelity prototypes. However, during this thesis, it was decided to
produce a high fidelity prototype that would include the design and functionality which
could be ready for code implementation.
The choice of developing a high fidelity prototype in this thesis, is due to BrainCapture’s
continuous development of their own application, which is at a high level in terms of the
user interface and user experience. The level therefore acts as a reference point. While
code implementation of the application is outside of the thesis scope, it is favorable for
the prototype to be high fidelity in order to meet the reference point. Furthermore, a high
fidelity prototype is needed to cover the complexity of the system.
8
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:16==
2.3.4
Usability testing
When conducting usability testing, an array of qualitative and quantitative testing methods
can be utilized. These testing methods are useful to accumulate data with the purpose of
optimizing user interface and user experience.
Qualitative testing methods, including think-aloud, interviews, critical incident, inter alia,
focus on identifying user related challenges by accumulating subjective user data. Oppo-
sitely, quantitative testing methods, including task completion time, error rate, question-
naires, inter alia, focus on gathering numerical data to evaluate the quality of the design
and functionality of the solution [12].
For this thesis, the think aloud qualitative testing method has been used to validate the
optimized application for each testing round. For quantitative testing methods, error rates,
and ratings on questionnaires methods have been used to track interaction and opinions
of the test users.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
9
==PAGE:17==
3
Research
In the 3 Research chapter, the first research question will be answered firstly by pre-
senting both the direct and indirect users who are impacted by the system. This will be
done by showing the typical diagnosis flow for a patient with suspected epilepsy in Den-
mark. Secondly, the user needs will be discovered through an analysis of qualitative data
consisting of expert interviews with three neurophysiology assistants, a specialized neu-
rology and neurophysiology doctor, and a medical laboratory technologist. Furthermore,
observations from two standard EEG scans and one standard control EEG scan con-
ducted at Sjællands Universitetshospital, Roskilde with three patients, aged 77, 37, and
17 years old respectively, will give a practical perspective. The main user needs will then
be categorized through two affinity maps and be presented.
3.1
Users
In Denmark, EEG systems, including the typical EEG setup, has one direct user and at
least four indirect users. When looking at the diagnosis flow (figure 3.1) for a patient with
suspected epilepsy, the typical impacted users are:
• Patient: The patient has experienced an episode resulting in a visit with their per-
sonal doctor. The patient might not be aware an episode has occurred as they may
have blacked out during. In these cases, it is usually a witness (i.e. family members,
friends, etc.) whom may assist the patient in describing what happened. It can also
be the case that the patient goes directly to the hospital if the episode is critical.
• Personal doctor: The personal doctor of the patient is usually a general practitioner
who knows about the medical history of the patient. The doctor is typically not spe-
cialized in epilepsy. While it is not mandatory, the personal doctor will usually write
a referral for an EEG scan.
• Neurophysiology assistant: The neurophysiology assistant is the one who con-
ducts the EEG scan on the patient and is therefore the main direct user of the sys-
tem. They typically work at the neurological department of a hospital or a specialized
epilepsy hospital. Once the scan has been conducted and evaluated by the assis-
tant, the results will be sent to a neurophysiologist.
• Neurophysiologist: The neurophysiologist receives the results and continues to
further analyze the patterns of the brainwaves to conclude whether epileptiform ac-
tivity has been detected. If abnormal activity has been detected, the updated results
with the conclusion will be sent to a neurologist. If abnormal activity has not been
detected, the patient will be called back for another type of EEG scan. This will
continue until a confident conclusion has been finalized.
10
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:18==
Figure 3.1: Typical diagnosis flow for an epilepsy suspected patient (A.8)
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
11
==PAGE:19==
• Neurologist: The neurologist receives the updated results and conclusion, evalu-
ates it, and delivers the final diagnosis to the patient.
When looking at the diagnosis flow (see figure 3.1), it is clear that the neurophysiology
assistant’s ability to deliver a quality scan is key to better the overall experience for the
patient. If a scan with low noise levels and well annotated artifacts is conducted, it will
decrease the chance of the patient getting called back for another EEG scan. Even more
so if epileptiform activity is detected but this is not in the control of the neurophysiology
assistants.
The flow shows the typical flow for a patient with suspected epilepsy living in Denmark. For
a patient living in a LMIC, the flow looks different. For LMICs, the diagnostic challenges
include lack in trained doctors and staff, relevant equipment, available AED, and diagnos-
tic facilities [1]. In order to counterattack these challenges, the existing resources in these
LMICs will have to cover the gap of the missing neurophysiology assistants amongst oth-
ers. This inevitably means, that the current EEG system will have to be modified so that
nurses with no specialized training in EEG or epilepsy will be able to conduct quality EEG
scans. Thus, different user needs for a nurse can be present.
3.2
User Needs
3.2.1
Expert interviews
In order to capture the user needs of the nurse for an MVP version of the system, five
expert interviews (A.1), including one neurophysiology assistant from Filadelfia epilepsi-
hospital and two from Sjællands Universitetshospital, Roskilde, a specialized neurology
and neurophysiology doctor from Rigshospitalet, and a medical laboratory technologist
also from Sjællands Universitetshospital, Roskilde, were conducted. While the interview
questions were adapted based on their roles, the general themes of the questions cov-
ered the general process of an EEG scan, the patient, and the EEG recording including
artifacts and biggest challenges.
The output of the interviews were categorized using the affinity mapping method, and the
main points can be read below.
EEG scan
Routine questions
Before the scan itself begins, the neurophysiology assistant will ask the patient what is
known as routine questions. While the questions are typically similar, the formulation of
the questions differ not only between hospitals but also between neurophysiology assis-
tants. At Filadelfia epilepsihospital, ”we have a routine questionnaire which we call the
DGR questionnaire (A.6) [...] which are just some questions we ask the patient.” (A.1.1)
They continue, ”I always ask about CPR number first [...] I need to know if they have
heart conditions, if they have had a blood clot or cerebral hemorrhage, if they have lung
12
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:20==
conditions, and for women, if they are possibly pregnant. [...] Lastly, I ask how often they
have experienced the episodes or seizures, and how many times they have had them in
total.” (A.1.1) At Filadelfia epilepsihospital, it is also protocol that ”the patient also signs
the paper with all these questions.” (A.1.1)
At Sjællands Universitetshospital, Roskilde , one neurophysiology assistant starts the ex-
amination with telling the patient ”you have had some episodes, or you have had a seizure
or you have something with your memory. (A.1.2) Another neurophysiology assistant asks
”about medical history, seizures and progress. I ask about things, when did it happen?
How often do you have the seizures? What did you feel? If they did not feel anything, if
someone has joined, what did they see? When was the last seizure?” (A.1.3) Also, the
medical laboratory technologist adds, ”we also ask when did you have your last seizure,
did you eat breakfast which is in regards to for example the hyperventilation.” (A.1.5)
The specialized neurology and neurophysiology doctor at Rigshospitalet emphasizes that
a ”clinical examination is also very important, a good medical history, also the family if
they have a child or from the patient themselves, a description of their episodes, how long
their episodes are, are there provoking factors, when did it start, can they remember the
episode, or were their conscious affected during the episode.” (A.1.4)
The habitual state of the patient is also important to know, as the neurophysiology assis-
tant asks the patient, ”if they feel like they usually do, so whether they are having a normal
day [...] because an EEG curve can look differently depending on how they feel.” (A.1.1)
The medical laboratory technologist agrees, as they ”always start by making a little note,
we all do this, where we indicate whether the patient is awake, asleep, are they confused,
are they oriented in time and place. Most importantly, are they in their habitual state.”
(A.1.5)
The examination usually consists of hyperventilation and photic stimulation, but based
on the answers the patient gives on the routine questions, some restrictions do apply.
One neurophysiology assistant says, ”as a baseline, we always do [the exercises] but I
think that it says in our instructions that people over 50 do not need to. But you usually
choose yourself, it is not restricted by saying if you are over 50 then you can not. But
when I receive a patient who has COPD or other types of lung diseases, then I do not
do it. (A.1.2) At the same hospital, another neurophysiology assistant clearly states ”no,
over 50 we do not hyperventilate. There are a lot of epilepsies, that are provoked, usually
epilepsies prone to children, and the older you are, the bigger the chance is for a blood
clot. For photic, we usually do not do photic stimulation on under one year. With both
hyper and photic, we do not provoke pregnant people, not because it would do something
but it could provoke a seizure if you have a specific type of epilepsy provoked by the lamp.
We are not interested in that with pregnant people.” (A.1.3)
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
13
==PAGE:21==
Equipment
As mentioned in the 3.1 Users section, it is the neurophysiology assistants who put the
equipment on the patient. The neurophysiology assistant at Filadelfia epilepsihospital has
”some loose electrodes, we have 27, which are some gold plated plates which we put on.”
(A.1.1) In order to put the loose electrodes on the scalp, they use ”some gel, which is rem-
iniscent of some face scrub because it has some small grains of sand things, which grinds
the scalp first. Then we have a salt water wax which we put on the individual electrode
and press it down on the scalp, so it can lead the signal better [...] it takes around 15
minutes to put on all the individual electrodes.” (A.1.1) Sometimes, the neurophysiology
assistant will choose another option, ”if not the loose electrodes, I have two options, then
we have a cap they can wear which is a little faster. [...] In our cap, there are only 19
electrodes, but if we know that it might be a temporal lobe epilepsy then we might put what
we call low-row electrodes on, which are three extra electrodes on each side, which we
normally put on with loose electrodes.” (A.1.1) When putting on the cap ”we also use the
scrubbing thing on the scalp, but then we put a more fluid gel with some salt in a syringe
which is then injected.” (A.1.1) Other than the EEG electrodes, ”there always has to be
ECG on, so that we also have the heart rhythm, and sometimes they also have to get
some attached to some muscles on the body.” (A.1.1)
The neurophysiology assistants at Sjællands Universitetshospital, Roskilde work similarly
with the equipment as one of them explains, ”we use 19 electrodes plus ground and
reference, and then six low-row. We go after the 10-20 system [...] it takes 15 minutes”
(A.1.3) About the extra low-row electrodes, another neurophysiology assistant explains
that ”it is our standard that we also include what we call low-row, but there can be some
situations, where a patient can not take it [...] We have some examinations where we
have decided not to put them on.” (A.1.2) In regards to the flexibility of options, the same
case as above is seen as, ”we can either choose a cap or loose electrodes, we decide
ourselves.” (A.1.2) Lastly, they also have extra electrodes, as they ”put ECG on and we
put an eye electrode on, and in some cases we can also put EMG electrodes on”. (A.1.2)
Exercises
The provocation exercises conducted during the scan are the same at the three hospitals.
Firstly, the neurophysiology assistant tells the patient, ”during the examination I will ask
you multiple times to open your eyes, close your eyes and blink with your eyes.” (A.1.2)
This is ”to see the difference in the EEG when they have open eyes and closed eyes, and
how it looks when they blink. It creates some weird waves so you have to see, it looks
like that, so that you do not confuse them with something else later. So they are laying
down and relaxing for approximately two minutes just with their eyes closed just to get an
overview of how their brain looks, how their activity looks.” (A.1.1) Secondly, ”there will
be one point where you will hyperventilate and this you will do for three minutes.” (A.1.2)
The neurophysiology assistant describes, ”during the hyperventilation, they have to do
a minimum of 20 breaths per minute, but there can be a difference in how well people
14
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:22==
are at doing it.” (A.1.2) The third exercise is photic stimulation, where ”I have a photo
lamp which blinks and it blinks with a very powerful light and here I will repeatably say
open your eyes and close your eyes.” (A.1.2) They continue, ”when I do photo, the way
it happens is that there are some different intervals, some of these photos they run for
10 seconds or 14 seconds or a whole minute. So for that I want for them to open their
eyes for approximately three to four seconds before the light starts to blink so that the
eyes have time to adjust and then we have four to five seconds where the light blinks
powerfully in the eyes before they can close them again.” (A.1.2)
During the exercises, one neurophysiology assistant underlines, that the patient ”can not
be tired, because if they drowse during the whole recording then it will be too slow. It will
become slower, it is between 8 and 12 Hz for an adult for example, who is laying down
with closed eyes and is awake. But if they drowse then it goes under the 8 Hz. If it is like
that the whole time then it is abnormal and we say that it is too slow for the age and level of
consciousness.” (A.1.3) The specialized neurology and neurophysiology doctor supports
this by saying, ”when they get drowsy or fall asleep the background [activity] will change.
Normally the background will become slower until they fall asleep and it becomes very
very slow, and it will show some special rhythms in the EEG.” (A.1.4)
When asked why these specific exercises were the standard for provoking epileptiform
activity, a neurophysiology assistant explains, ”light stimulation because there are a lot
who are sensitive to light and hyperventilation because there are a lot of kids who are
sensitive to that. It is simply to provoke [...] we really want to see something in the EEG.”
(A.1.1) The specialized neurology and neurophysiology doctor adds, ”in a routine EEG
we usually stimulate the patient, we stimulate by blinking with some light, hyperventilation
and expect that the patient will fall asleep because most epilepsy types will accentuate
the pathological findings where the low-frequent changes are and also the epileptiform
changes during these stimulation periods.” (A.1.4)
Once the provocation exercises have been completed, the patient ”is allowed for the rest
of the time, so approximately 14 to 15 minutes, to lay down with closed eyes and relax. It
is a part of the 30 minutes.” (A.1.1) When asked when the patient is allowed to sleep, a
neurophysiology assistant states, ”they are allowed to, they just can not sleep the whole
time, they can sleep at the end.” (A.1.3) But if the patient falls asleep in the beginning, ”we
just let them sleep. There just has to be a point where we know that they are completely
awake because that is what we judge based on to see if it corresponds to their age.”
(A.1.3)
Flexible System
The EEG scan in itself follows a standard procedure but it can change according to the
patient. This is what neurophysiology assistants claim as one states, ”it can happen that
we have a child who does not want to lay still, is running around and playing, where you
just have to accept, okay they have on the electrodes, that is fine. It is also not always
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
15
==PAGE:23==
the case that they will want to be photo stimulated or get them to hyperventilate. So there
are many times where we kind of deviate from the standard” (A.1.1) They continue, ”you
never know who is coming through the door so you always have to be quick and say, now
we are doing it differently” (A.1.1).
It is standard protocol to put different kinds of electrodes on the patient in order to have a
multi-modal based examination, but ”we have some examinations where we choose not
to put it on” (A.1.2). These cases especially include children where ”we are happy that
we even get the cap on [...] and we probably do not get the extra ones on the cheeks on
either.” (A.1.3)
As mentioned above, another protocol is to ask the patient different routine questions
before the scan, but ”sometimes their conscious can be so affected, that it is no use to ask
them some questions they can not answer.” (A.1.2) Therefore, the system ”is extremely
flexible because it is based on what the patient can contribute with.” (A.1.2) It is also
possible ”if we have time to expand the examination if 10 more minutes or half an hour
more is needed” (A.1.1). The neurophysiology assistants emphasize that ”we always tell
the patient that it is their choice so if they do not want to continue they can say no and we
stop” (A.1.1) as the exercises ”are voluntary, both hyperventilation and photic stimulation,
it is not something they have to do.” (A.1.3)
Despite not following the exact protocol one neurophysiology assistant states, ”I can still
see something in the EEG even if it does not follow the standard procedure.” (A.1.1)
Overall, another neurophysiology assistant expresses ”in reality, we take what we can
get.” (A.1.2)
Quality Control
Artifacts
Artifacts can be divided in two groups based on the source, namely physiologic and non-
physiologic [1]. The physiologic artifacts are typically caused by the patient which are
therefore characterized as internal factors and include muscle and eye movements inter
alia [1]. On the contrary, nonphysiologic artifacts are mostly caused by external factors
which include the environment, equipment, etc [1].
Physiologic artifacts are generally affecting all recordings, a neurophysiology assistant
explains, ”in the ideal examination, the patient is laying down and is relaxing totally with
closed eyes without clenching their muscles or anything else. But most often you acciden-
tally smile, move, blink, or clench” (A.1.1). Another neurophysiology assistant agrees and
says that the most typical artifact they see ”is when the patient clenches causing muscle
activity. And if you blink too much, then it will cause blinking movements” (A.1.3) They
add, ”there can be a lot of movement artifacts for kids. So it is necessary for the parents to
be included and entertain as best as possible. Because if they cry for all 30 minutes, then
we can not really use it for anything. There is simply too much noise, if they are wiggling
and crying” (A.1.3). When asked which artifacts they most typically see, the specialized
16
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:24==
neurology and neurophysiology doctor jokes, ”I could hold a lecture about that [...] pulse
artifacts, ECG artifacts, blinking artifacts, eye movement artifacts, muscle artifacts, big
movement artifacts, pacifier artifacts with toddlers.” (A.1.4) They further underline why
artifacts have a big effect on the EEG saying, ”because the muscle movements create
big swings. The EEG shows a little swing in the size of a micro volt.” (A.1.4) Therefore,
one neurophysiology assistant explains, ”the EEG is prettier when they have their eyes
closed, so during the whole recording, you will want them to have their eyes closed as far
as possible.” (A.1.1)
Nonphysiologic artifacts also plague recordings one neurophysiology assistant complains
stating that ”electrical noise is a problem.” (A.1.3) Another neurophysiology assistant con-
curs when expressing that the most typical artifact they see ”is noise, that is noise coming
from the outside, which affects the signals causing them to look more... they do not look
that clean, they look more murky, grainy.” (A.1.2) The medical laboratory technologist
points out that ”basically the impedance just need to be consistent and not vary too much,
so that you do not have one which is on five and one which is on 15 because it can also
give some noise artifacts” (A.1.5).
Artifacts can cause misinterpretation, but the medical laboratory technologist explains
”in order to not misinterpret it as some seizure activity, then it can be important to write
events.” (A.1.5)
During the examination, while also directing the patient, the neurophysiology assistant
will annotate different events in the EEG to help resolve some of the misinterpretation.
The medical laboratory technologist describes ”there are some typical events which are
auto defined, those we use a lot. Then we have this OBS where we can write ourselves
so free text [...] Then we put them in throughout when needed, it is also possible to put
them in after when we have done the recording [...] we sit and monitor all the time [...] we
will go in and add something if something happens during.” (A.1.5) They add, ”of course,
we always annotate when they have their eyes open and when they are closed.” (A.1.5)
About annotation, one neurophysiology assistant also says, ”we write in the curve, for
example, ”clenches jaw” and then I ask the patient to open their mouth slightly. I also
write this in the curve, so ”encourage to relax better” and ”putting sandbag on eyes”. This
we write in the curve. Then the neurologist can see, that we have tried to see if we can
make it better.” (A.1.3)
Review of EEG
After the examination has been conducted, the patient is cleaned up and sent home,
”then I go in and look at the examination right after or at least on the same day.” (A.1.1)
To review the EEG, ”we have this SCORE program where we are asked some questions
[...] for example what is the background activity? What is the frequency? What is the
amplitude? Where is something located? Is there something? Do you see sleep? Then
you also have the opportunity to add comments if there for example is something you
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
17
==PAGE:25==
can not answer using these things” says one of the neurophysiology assistants (A.1.2).
The medical laboratory technologist also mentions ”the impedance measurements. It will
always be saved and if you measure the impedance during the recording, it will also save
it so that you can go in and see exactly what the impedance was at the given time, and that
is just for quality assurance and to document that all the electrodes were stuck.” (A.1.5)
When looking at the EEG for evaluation, a neurophysiology assistant explains ”what I am
looking for are some specific patterns which pop up on the EEG. They have a very special
shape where I can then read, okay this is something epileptic. Then I can see which
electrode it popped up on and then I can say it was frontal or occipital. So it is some very
special patterns I look for.” (A.1.1) The specialized neurology and neurophysiology doctor
somewhat describes the evaluation of the EEG as a quest for answers since ”almost
everyone is a little different. With some patients you can see that it is a muscle artifact,
and this is something which has graphical change in the EEG, and you have to find out, this
is not epileptic but then what is it? Is it a tic? Are there some stereotypical movements? Is
there some dystonia? What do they have?” (A.1.4) This is where the doctor emphasizes
the importance of a multi-modal examination, as they state, ”it is always a multi-modal
recording, we have ECG, EEG, and EMG, so that we have a multi-model registration and
then connect the things and try to diagnose.” (A.1.4)
A neurophysiology assistant adds that lastly when the patient ”has been here and you
have figured out which diagnosis they have then they sometimes come in for a control
EEG, which is just half an hour where they come in where you just see how it looks”
(A.1.1).
Synchronized Video
Both a neurophysiology assistant and the specialized neurology and neurophysiology
doctor strongly agree that video recording of the patient during the examination is im-
portant. The neurophysiology assistant explains that the patient ”is video recorded, this is
always with all EEG’s [...] if a seizure were to happen then the doctor can also see what is
going on, when the doctor looks it through later.” (A.1.3) They continue, ”it is always good
to also have video on, because in the old days then we only did something called Oxford
monitoring where they went home with the electrodes on and then we could not see if they
scratched themselves and this actually caused something rhythmic on the EEG which we
then had to judge, is that focal temporallobe seizure or is it because the patient scratched
themselves? So that is also very important.” (A.1.3)
The specialized neurology and neurophysiology doctor also makes it clear that video
recording is ”very important, because a small movement or some epileptiform movements
sometimes with seizures there are so many muscle artifacts that you would not be able
to see anything on the EEG. Therefore, video, more seizures, how stereotypical they are,
are they the same or are they all different, this gives a lot. Is it just, for example, that a
child is clenching their entire body or is it epileptiform? You need the video to see the
18
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:26==
difference.” (A.1.4)
Challenges
Patient behaviour
A neurophysiology assistant tells that the biggest challenge for them during an exami-
nation, ”is always the unknown factor in regards to what kind of patient you get. So the
biggest challenge is if you get a patient which in someway deviates from the standard and
you have to accommodate to the patient, think outside the box. If there is someone who
does not want to, has to be persuaded, or a child then I would say those are the biggest
challenges.” (A.1.1)
The two other neurophysiology assistants agree in the child being a big challenge as one
of them describes their challenges as ”children who feel uneasy or children who have
had a bad experience at the hospital before, because then they are probably primed to
believing that when I come in with white pants and a blue shirt, then this is going to be bad.
I think that is probably the biggest challenge.” (A.1.2) The last neurophysiology assistant
echoes in saying, ”it is probably when children do not want to. It is because the adults
they come in and they know why they need to do this and they want to but the children
do not always know.” (A.1.3)
Seizure handling
While the term seizure is known as a phenomenon which affects people with epilepsy
because of abnormal brain activity [1], neurophysiology assistants tend to use the word
episode in front of patients instead, ”since it is not always something epileptic [patients]
come with. Calling it a seizure will feel very overwhelming for some, to be told that they
have had a seizure cause often you are not conscious when having a seizure so many do
not acknowledge that they have epilepsy.” (A.1.1) When asked how often patients have
a seizure during the scan, the neurophysiology assistant answers ”it happens that people
have seizures. For the standard scan, we preferably do not want them to have a seizure.”
(A.1.1) They continue, ”I can easily figure out if they have epilepsy or not without them
having a seizure.” (A.1.1) Another neurophysiology assistant agrees by saying, ”we are
not interested in people having a seizure here. We are just interested in seeing where
this activity is located and if it fits with the seizures they have had.” (A.1.2) A third neuro-
physiology assistant specifies, ”absences are okay, you just keep going, but if I see in the
EEG that a seizure is building up, then we stop [the exercise] before we get the cramps if
we can. It rarely happens that we provoke a generalised tonic-clonic seizure.” (A.1.3)
Despite the intention of not wanting to provoke a seizure during the examination, it does
occur at times, and it created a challenge for one neurophysiology assistant as they ex-
plain, ”something I thought was really difficult to begin with was if the patient got a seizure,
it happens even though we do not want it to. Or if we see something in the EEG, we have
to test them. We see something in the EEG which looks epileptic and then I do some
things or ask the patient some questions to test their consciousness, whether they can
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
19
==PAGE:27==
hear me, whether they can see me, different things.” (A.1.1)
The specialized neurology and neurophysiology doctor describes a different challenge
also in regards to seizures, as ”sometimes with normal EEG, the patient also has seizures
at night. Most seizures happen at night, but the patient comes during work hours so we
can not see anything.” (A.1.4)
Other illnesses
Figuring out if the episode the patient has had is epilepsy is the main goal for all the roles,
but sometimes the catalyst is something else. ”There is also something called PNES. It is
not epileptic but it is seizures which are psychologically triggered”, one neurophysiology
assistant states (A.1.2). Another neurophysiology assistant explains ”it can be pressure
because of school or other things so that you start to faint and it can also be anxiety. Then
[the patients] also come to us for a clarification [...] Then it is another road they have to
go with the treatment.” (A.1.3)
Lastly, the specialized neurology and neurophysiology doctor states, that ”an episode can
also have a cardiological trigger.” (A.1.4)
3.2.2
Standard EEG scan observations
Through a second affinity map, three main categories have been seen when looking at the
EEG observations (A.2); Patient information, equipment, and exercises. A fourth section
would be review of scan but this was not a part of the observations due to time limitations.
Patient information
During the patient information section of the examination, the main goal for the neurophys-
iology assistants is to get a preliminary overview of the patient. In all three observations
(A.2), one of the first things the assistants would ask the patient for was their CPR num-
ber. Thenceforth, the assistant would ask questions with the aim of assessing the habitual
state of the patient by asking them if they know why they are having the examination and
how they feel. Next, the patient is asked whether they have eaten and at what time, how
they have slept and for how long. In regards to their seizure history, the assistants ask
about their most recent seizure and their seizure frequency. Lastly, the patient is asked
about their medical history and medication.
Equipment
Following the patient information is the equipment. The neurophysiology assistants have
typically prepared all the equipment beforehand if time allows it. Some of the assistants
choose to show the equipment to the patients while others do not but all of them explain
each piece of the equipment before equipping the patient. Regardless whether the as-
sistant has chosen to go with a cap or loose electrodes, they all measure the patient’s
head firstly. Of the equipment, the EEG electrodes are in all the observations attached
firstly using two kinds of gel, saltwater gel and NuPrep gel. While putting on the elec-
trodes, the connection for each electrode is checked using a device which is connected
20
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:28==
to the electrodes and gives a visual overview of the impedance and connection of the
electrodes. The device uses small lights for each electrode which are all switched on
where light means no connection/high impedance whereas no light means a good con-
nection/low impedance (A.2.1). Secondly, the ECG electrodes are attached and lastly,
the eye electrode while the connections are also controlled. The neurophysiology assis-
tants utilize the time while putting on all the equipment to also explain the examination to
the patient.
Exercises
Once the patient is equipped, the neurophysiology assistant asks the patient to close their
eyes. They then start the video and then the recording. The assistants annotate level of
consciousness and mark down the used equipment. Then they introduce, initiate, and
guide the patient through different exercises; Eyes, hyperventilation, and photic stimula-
tion.
Before, during and after the exercises, the neurophysiology assistant annotates com-
mands (i.e. eyes open, eyes closed, and blink) using both predefined and free text anno-
tations, annotates exercises and they also annotate deviations which cause artifacts. One
neurophysiology assistant states, “in general, it is better to annotate if in doubt rather than
not doing it” (A.2.1). In the first observation of the 77 year old women (A.2.1), 15 annota-
tions were made during the exercises and 23 annotations were made in total throughout
the examination (note that because of age restrictions, the hyperventilation exercise was
skipped which means that more annotations would have been made if the exercise had
been conducted as well). In the second observation of the 37 year old women (A.2.2)
43 annotations were made during the exercises and 55 annotations were made in total
throughout the examination, and in the third observation of the 17 year old male (A.2.3),
50 annotations were made during the exercises and 76 annotations were made in total
throughout the examination.
Once the exercises are completed, the neurophysiology assistant asks the patient to keep
their eyes closed and relax for the remainder of the examination. The assistant will fo-
cus harder on possible changes in the EEG due to potential spikes as a result of the
provocation exercises.
3.3
Sub-conclusion
Six main themes of user needs have been categorized. EEG scan, where routine ques-
tions, equipment, and exercises were the essential parts of the examination. This was
also supported by the EEG observations where the same themes were seen. It was
also clearly emphasized that the system had to be flexible in order to accommodate the
patient. The use of the system’s flexibility was especially present in the third EEG obser-
vation where the patient fell asleep in the beginning of the scan and then later when the
neurophysiology assistant was not satisfied with how the patient was performing during
the photic stimulation exercise, and therefore stopped it and tried hyperventilation.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
21
==PAGE:29==
Quality control in terms of annotating artifacts and reviewing the EEG are a focal point for
the neurophysiology assistant as a preliminary analysis is key to bettering the final results.
The amount of annotations made during the EEG observations shows how important it is
for the user to be able to easily and efficiently annotate in the system. The synchronised
video also proved to be a valuable tool to asses the examination.
In terms of challenges, patient behaviour and seizure handling, which inevitably is also
connected to the patient, were chosen since the patient was described as the unknown
factor. Lastly, it was discovered that at times seizures were caused by other sources than
epilepsy, and then the neurophysiology assistant would have to take a stand.
These main themes for user needs will all influence the development of an MVP version
of the system with a nurse as the main direct user.
22
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:30==
4
Analysis
Before an optimized version of the application can be justified, it is necessary to asses
the state of the user interface and user experience of the BrainCapture solution. The 4
Analysis chapter, answers the second research question and will be done in two parts.
In part one, the strengths and weaknesses of the user interface and experience of the
BrainCapture solution will be assessed through an expert user experience review moti-
vated by Peter Morville’s User Experience Honeycomb framework. The framework has
seven sections; Usable, useful, desirable, findable, accessible, credible, and valuable,
where different tools will be used to analyze if and how the solution satisfies the individual
sections.
In the second part, a SWOT analysis focusing on the strengths, weaknesses, opportuni-
ties, and threats of both internal and external factors concerning the overall system will
be examined.
4.1
User Experience Review
BrainCapture is currently working on their application (V1), and the development of the
application is based on a prototype which was delivered by an external company. The
scope of the prototype is bigger, and therefore includes more features and screens than
the developed BrainCapture application in its current state. But with continuing develop-
ment, BranCapture aims to reach the same scope as the prototype in terms of the user
interface and user experience. Thus, the prototype will be the main subject of the analy-
sis. However, results from conducted user tests and a focus group will also be included
throughout the analysis, and these were not conducted with the prototype, but with the
aforementioned BrainCapture application (V1).
Since receiving the prototype from the external company, an user experience expert has
never reviewed it. Therefore, an user experience review is conducted on the prototype
focusing on the User Experience Honeycomb framework.
In the Honeycomb, the following tools and materials will be used to assess each section:
• Usable: User experience inspection focusing on Jakob Nielsen’s 10 Usability Heuris-
tics for User Interface Design as well as a focus group (A.3), and user tests (A.4).
• Useful: Expert interviews (A.1), EEG observations (A.2), and user tests (A.4).
• Desirable: Schneiderman’s 8 Golden Design Rules.
• Findable: User tests on the BrainCapture application (A.4).
• Accessible: 7 Principles of Universal Design.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
23
==PAGE:31==
• Credible: The Elements of Value Pyramid.
• Valuable: An assessment of the accumulated findings of the other sections.
4.1.1
Usable
An user experience inspection, focusing on Jakob Nielsen’s 10 Usability Heuristics for
User Interface Design, will be done on the BrainCapture prototype with the aim of adjudg-
ing usability strengths in terms of what works well for the user, and weaknesses in terms
of what could possibly cause issues for the user. Furthermore, the results of a focus group
conducted on the application, with 11 participants including neurophysiology assistants,
laboratory technologists, and medical students (A.3) and an user test with nurses (A.4)
will also be used to adjudge whether the prototype fulfills the usable section of the User
Experience Honeycomb.
Strengths
Visibility of system status
Exercises
Figure 4.1: Connection status
During the ”Guided exercises” flow, the user has the ability to check ”Connection status”
of the EEG cap, the amplifier, and the ECG by clicking a button with an EEG cap icon on
it (see figure 4.1). This will open a sheet component including a visual presentation of the
EEG electrodes where the electrodes are green if the connection is good, yellow if it is
average, and white if it is disconnected. For the amplifier and ECG, their connection status
is also shown by a light with the same color coordination. This gives an easy overview of
the system’s connection status.
General
24
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:32==
A progress bar is present in both the ”Pre-interview”, ”Prepare Equipment”, ”Guided ex-
ercises”, and ”Evaluation” flows (see figure 4.1 above the connection status button). This
bar gives an easy overview of the progress which is useful in practice during the exami-
nation for the user to help guide the patient throughout.
Match between system and the real world
Prepare Equipment
Figure 4.2: Overview of EEG electrodes
In the ”Prepare Equipment” flow on the ”Connect electrodes” screen, the user is presented
with a visual overview of the EEG electrodes (see figure 4.2) which are mirrored by how
the user sees the EEG electrodes in the real world which helps guide the user in dealing
with them.
Exercises
During the ”Guided exercises”, the user is presented with different icons representing the
exercise they have to ask the patient to do (see figure 4.3). This gives the user a visual
guidance on top of the help text presented on the screens.
User control and freedom
General
Throughout the application, a skip functionality is present for the user (see figure 4.3 at
the bottom of the screen). This respects the control and freedom of the user to freely
move forward in the application.
Consistency and standards
Exercises
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
25
==PAGE:33==
Figure 4.3: Exercise icons
During the ”Guided exercises” flow, the exercises are consistent in design and functionality
(see figure 4.3), creating a higher learnability for the user, making it easier to navigate
through the exercises.
Figure 4.4: Orange screen shown after exercise completion
Once an exercise is completed, the user is presented with an orange screen (see figure
4.4). Since this screen is consistently presented after the completion of every single ex-
ercise, it affects the user subconsciously thus leading them to associate that the orange
screen means that the exercise is completed.
Aesthetic and minimalist design
26

Pre-interview
Figure 4.5: Pre-interview Answers screen
The ”Pre-interview Answers” screen is seen after all the questions in the ”Pre-interview”
flow have been answered (see figure 4.5). This screen presents the inputted answers
from the user in a simple and clean way as well as easily giving the option of editing if
needed.
Help users recognize, diagnose, and recover from errors
Alert messages
Figure 4.6: Different alert messages seen throughout the flow
Throughout the application different messages can be triggered. During the ”Pre-interview”
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX

==PAGE:35==
flow, the user has to ask the question ”Are you pregnant?” to the patient. If the patient an-

guidelines” (see figure 4.6). This warning helps the user in conducting an examination

The same instance is seen for the questions, ”Have you had any brain surgery?” and ”Do

Another one of these error messages which help the user recover from an error is ”Elec-

Help and documentation
Prepare Equipment & Side menu
In the ”Prepare Equipment” flow, a help functionality in the shape of a hyperlink button is
present throughout giving the user an easy access to help if needed (see figure 4.2 above
the inactive Next button).
Figure 4.7: Help located in the side menu
Furthermore, the help functionality is seen in the side menu (see figure 4.7) meaning the
user has access to help at any time throughout the application.
Weaknesses
Visibility of system status
Prepare Equipment
During the ”Prepare Equipment” flow, the user is asked to connect to the amplifier by
clicking a dropdown menu and choosing the correct amplifier (see figure 4.8). Then, the

Exercises
Figure 4.9: Displayed recording time
On the first screen of the ”Guided exercises” flow, it is stated that the recording time has

User control and freedom
Exercises
When the user is going through the ”Guided exercises” flow, they have no back navigation
(see figure 4.3) which takes away the control from the user as they can not freely move
back in the flow.
Consistency and Standards
Home
Figure 4.10: Card component with different functionalities
The ”Start Epilepsy Scan” card on the ”Home” screen is clickable, and once the user has
clicked it, they are taken to the next screen where another card, ”Preparation”, is present
(see figure 4.10). But this card is not clickable and to start the scan, the user has to click
the ”Get started” in the bottom of the screen. This creates an inconsistency in the use of
card components.
Pre-interview
The alert messages seen in the ”Pre-interview” do not follow the semantic color scheme
(see figure 4.6), thus the messages do not look like typical alert messages which the user
might be used to.
Prepare Equipment
On the screen where the user is asked to ”Put on EEG cap”, there is a sudden relocation
of the ”Next” button from the previous screen (see figure 4.11) which might confuse the
user and lead them to click the ”Previous” button instead.
Exercises

Figure 4.12: Change in buttons
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
31
==PAGE:39==
Once the exercises are finished, the user sees a screen where they can either start the
evaluation, or finish the recording. The issue here, is with the ”Finish recording” button
which was previously a skip button which could cause misclicks (see figure 4.12).
General
Figure 4.13: Different versions for a skip functionality
A skip functionality is present on the ”Pre-interview”, ”Prepare Equipment”, ”Guided ex-
ercises”, and ”Evaluation” flows, where some are in the shape of a button while most are
present as hyperlinks (see figure 4.13). In total, there are three different looks for the
skip functionality. While the three different looks for the skip functionality have different
purposes, namely skip exercise, skip question, or just skip, it can be overwhelming for the
user to have multiple different presentations. For example, they could be interpreted dif-
ferently, e.g. as one presentation having more impact than another and therefore create
doubt.
Similarly, it seems like a close functionality is also presented differently throughout the
prototype (see figure 4.14). On a screen in the ”Prepare Equipment” flow, an ”X” icon is
present, and on the ”Home” screen, a ”Cancel” button is seen at the top right corner. While
an ”X” icon and a ”Cancel” button have different purposes in practice, in this prototype they
seem to serve the same purpose of closing down an action.
Error Prevention
Pre-interview
From question 12 to 13 there is a change in the type of question (see figure 4.15). This
is only indicated by a change in the small text which goes from ”ASK THE PATIENT” to
”CLINICAL QUESTION”. In a real life setting, this might not be clear enough for the user,
and they might mistakenly ask the wrong type of question, more so if they are inexperi-
