enced.
Exercises
Figure 4.16: Reporting events
During the ”Guided exercises” flow, the user has the ability to ”Report event” by clicking
a button with an exclamation icon on it. This will open a sheet component with different
predefined events and a ”Free note” option. The user then clicks on the orange ”Report”
button on the right side of the event they want to report (see figure 4.16). A challenge
here, is that the user has no way of removing the reported event which might cause false
positive reported events.
Flexibility and Efficiency of use
Pre-interview
In the ”Pre-interview” flow, the user is asked to put in the date of birth of the patient (see
figure 4.17). This is done through four input fields and one checkbox. The year, month,
and day are all individually selected through three individual scrollable modal date pickers
and then clicking ”Ok” each time. Then, the actual age has to be inputted by the user in a
text input field, and click the checkbox if the age is an estimation. This accumulates to 11
interactions between the user and the application, only to input the date of birth and age
of the patient. This is inefficient and time consuming.
Aesthetic and Minimalist Design
Recordings & Sidemenu
In the top left corner of each screen there is a menu, where the user can click to see the
”Recording History” (see figure 4.18). In this screen there is an icon which indicates that
the recording file has been uploaded to the cloud. In addition, there is also a ”Status:
34
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:42==
Figure 4.17: Inputting of date of birth
Figure 4.18: Recordings screen
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
35
==PAGE:43==
Uploaded” text. It seems redundant and space consuming to have both of these which
ultimately have the same purpose for the user.
As mentioned, the menu is placed in the top left corner of each screen, however the
content of the menu is not critical to have on each screen (see figure 4.18). The main
content which could be necessary for a user at all times would be the ”Help”, but for each
exercise there is a hyperlinked ”Help” button which serves this purpose.
Also, the cloud icon and the upload text are orange (see figure 4.18). The orange color is
used a lot throughout the application, also for call-to-action buttons, and is very present on
this screen. Not only the icon and the upload text use the orange, also the buttons ”Finish
Pre-interview” and ”View Evaluation” use it which gives off an overwhelming feeling, and
takes away the impact a color can have for a user to navigate on the screen.
Pre-interview
There is a total of 13 questions in the ”Pre-interview” flow, and each question is on an in-
dividual screen with between two to five interactions each needed from the user including
clicking the ”Next question” button (see figure 4.17). This could make the application feel
long and cumbersome.
Furthermore, there are two ”Skip” buttons on the same screen, one at the top right corner
called ”Skip” and one below the question called ”Skip question” (see figure 4.17). While
these two buttons might have different functionalities and purposes, it might create con-
fusion for a user to see two different skip options on the same screen.
Exercises
Figure 4.19: ”Go to exercise” screen
On the ”Go to exercise” screens in the ”Guided exercises” flow, the screen is busy with a
lot of small text which might not be noticeable for some users (see figure 4.19).
36
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:44==
Furthermore, the time of the exercise is stated twice (see figure 4.19), and once the exer-
cise is finished an excessive amount of text is seen on the screen. Both these examples,
show unnecessities which challenge a minimalist design.
Evaluation
Figure 4.20: ”You’re done!” screen
On the last screen in the ”Evaluation” flow, the user sees a ”You’re done!” screen (see fig-
ure 4.20). The screen is crowded with a title, some text, a card with text and a button, and
two more buttons, which might overwhelm the user. Furthermore, both the background
and the call-to-action button are the same orange which breaks the impact of the color
usage.
User tests and focus group results
On 6/9 2022, a focus group interview consisting of five neurophysiology assistants, four
medical laboratory technologists, and two medical students, who work with EEG and
epilepsy, took place at Sjællands Universitetshospital, Roskilde (A.3). The participants
were given a presentation of BrainCapture, and their equipment including the application
(V1). After the presentation, the participants were allowed to roughly interact with the
application, and were asked to rate their opinion on application guidance, usability and
learnability on a scale from ”Strongly disagree” to ”Strongly agree”. (Note that two partic-
ipants had to leave before the voting session resulting in 9 participants who voted at the
end).
On 14/9, 21/9, and 22/9 2022, user tests including five nurses, who had no prior training
with EEG or epilepsy, were facilitated by the usability expert at BrainCapture (A.4). The
tests were conducted on the BrainCapture system including equipment and application
(V1). The nurses were asked to conduct three scans on individuals who acted as patients
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
37
==PAGE:45==
(15 individuals in total). The nurses were firstly given a rough introduction to epilepsy,
seizures, and artifacts, then to the system. Lastly, a demo of how to complete a scan
was conducted by the usability expert and presented to the nurses. (Note that A.4 only
shows the results of three out of five of the participants since the last two participants were
documented by another facilitator).
Results from both these studies will be used to adjudge usability strengths and weak-
nesses from the user’s point of view.
Strengths
During the focus group, the participants had positive thoughts to share about the system.
This is reflected in their voting (A.3) where all participants voted either ”Agree” or ”Strongly
agree” when asked if they feel guided well when using the application, and whether the
application was easy to use, thus agreeing that the application had easy usability from
their perspective. For the statement, ”It requires a lot of training to learn how to use the
app”, all participants voted ”Strongly disagree”, while voting ”Strongly agree” when asked
if most people could quickly learn how to use the application, which shows that they found
the application to have simple learnability.
Similar tendencies are seen in the user tests (A.4). In the first scan of the first test user
(A.4.1), the user intuitively uses the application to check the electrode connection. As
mentioned, the match between system and the real world heuristic guides the user. Fur-
thermore, the user reads the text included in the exercises aloud to help themselves learn
how to perform the exercise and also to the patient for them to create an understanding as
well. The second user (A.4.2) also had the same experience in easily checking the elec-
trode connection when asked to do so and using the exercise text when interacting with
the patient. The user also easily noted artifacts, and found the application both intuitive
and informative.
On the second scan, it was seen that the second user (A.4.2) had managed to correct
errors made during the first scan, i.e. correctly turning on the amplifier and remembering
to connect it to the cap.
The third scan showed usability for all the users. The first user (A.4.1) managed to cor-
rectly start the process, connected the cap and amplifier, and applying ECG after failing
to do so correctly on the first two scans. They went through the exercises smoothly and
easily navigated through the application. The second user (A.4.2) also managed to start
in the correct flow and apply ECG in the correct order. The third user (A.4.3) easily con-
nected the ground and reference electrodes after seemingly finding this confusing on the
first two scans.
Conclusively, the first user (A.4.1) finds that the application is quick to learn, simple, and
pleasant. The second user (A.4.2) found it easy to annotate artifacts, and that the appli-
cation guided them well overall. The third user (A.4.3) also found the application simple
and friendly for the patient, and stated that once the application was figured out it was
38
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:46==
uncomplicated to navigate throughout the examination.
Weaknesses
The participants of the focus group (A.3) agreed that the application was missing back
navigation. They expressed a wish of wanting to be able to freely navigate through the
flow of the application if needed. This is also a clear violation of the user control and
freedom heuristic as previously mentioned.
For the user tests (A.4), challenges with the flow of the application seemed to be the
biggest issue for the first user (A.4.1). Firstly, the user forgot to connect the EEG cap to
the amplifier, and then they reversed the order of applying the ECG resulting them to try
again. Another challenge was the report event icon not being clear for the user as well
as the text seen on the ”Relaxation” screens of the application which was found to be
confusing.
The application flow was also a grand challenge for the second user (A.4.2) as they in-
tuitively wanted to measure the cap size before starting the application, forgot to connect
the cap to the amplifier, accidentally skipped the screen where they are asked to put gel
in all electrodes, and lastly, putting on the ECG device in the incorrect order.
This theme was also seen with the third user (A.4.3) as they also started measuring the
cap size before turning on the application, and accidentally skipped the ECG entirely
without noticing causing the first examination to be run uni-modal. More challenges were
present for the third user during their first scan. Firstly, a usability issue is apparent as
the user did not find it intuitive to tap the card to start the examination causing them to
click and look in the menu in confusion until they were eventually helped by the facilitator.
The design of the application was also a dominant challenge as the user almost skipped a
”Relaxation” step in the flow, as the white card was more noticeable on the screen for the
user. Furthermore in the ”Relaxation” flow, the user is asked to have the patient relax for
10 minutes but notes that it says that the duration for the next exercise is approximately
three minutes leading them to mistakenly ask the patient to relax for three minutes instead.
Lastly, when reaching the photic stimulation exercise, which the users were asked to skip,
the user is unsure how to skip the exercise and intuitively clicks an inactive ”Next” button.
These instances further underline that the application violates the aesthetic and minimalist
design heuristic as mentioned previously.
The challenges with the application flow transcend to the second scan for all the users,
and also to the third scan for the third user. Thus, a challenge with usability is apparent
in the flow of the application.
Conclusively, the first user (A.4.1) finds that the connection between the cap and the
amplifier is missing explanation, and also commented that the timing of when to report
an artifact to be difficult to know. The second user (A.4.2) found the lack of freedom in
going back to also be a weakness in the application. Moreover, the application flow was
challenging as well for the second user mentioning that the timer for each exercise would
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
39
==PAGE:47==
start before they felt ready to start the exercise (note, that while the prototype allows the
user to start the timer by themselves, this is not the case for the application (V1)). This
could also be examples of violating the user control and freedom heuristic as the timer
would start before the user would want it to causing them to feel out of control. The third
user (A.4.3) echoes this, mentioning that they felt stressed when the timer started by
itself. They also mention that it is easier and more comfortable for them to put on all the
equipment first despite what is presented in the flow of the application. Lastly, the user
points out that colors of the application are misleading as they put focus on the wrong
things hinting at the ”Relaxation” screens.
To sum up, it was seen through the user experience inspection and the results of the
focus group and the user tests that while the prototype is generally usable, it has its short-
comings. The prototype violated the visibility of system status, user control and freedom,
consistency and standards, error prevention, flexibility and efficiency of use, and aesthetic
and minimalist usability heuristics causing usability weaknesses. Some of these weak-
nesses were also present in the results of the focus group and the user tests. Here, the
lack of back navigation and overall flow of the application (V1) were the biggest issues.
This results in the usable section of the User Experience Honeycomb not being satisfied.
4.1.2
Useful
In the 3.2 User Needs section, user needs were established through the expert interviews
(A.1) alongside the standard EEG scan observations (A.2). While other user needs are
generally satisfied, it is noticeable, through the 4.1.1 Usable subsection, that two of
the user needs, namely flexible system and quality control, are especially challenged.
Therefore, these two needs will be utilized to adjudge whether the application fulfills the
useful section of the User Experience Honeycomb. The results from the user tests (A.4)
will also be included to highlight the findings.
Flexible System
An important user need discovered was for the system to be flexible in order for the user
to best accommodate the patient. In the application, it is possible to restart an exercise
if the user is unsatisfied with the performance or for other reasons. It is also an option to
completely skip an exercise or any other step in the application. This freedom delivers on
the need for a flexible system.
Where the application lacks in terms of flexibility is the missing back navigation which con-
fines the user in only being able to follow the application linearly. This was also apparent
in the focus group (A.3) and user tests (A.4) where multiple users were asking for back
navigation. The linearity continues in the navigational structure of the application so that
the flow is already decided for the user, thus the flexibility of choosing the order of exer-
cises is taken away from the user. This for example does not accommodate, the instance
seen in the EEG observations (A.2.3) where the neurophysiology assistant chose to start
with the photic stimulation exercise before the hyperventilation exercise.
40
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:48==
Quality Control
The user can report an event throughout the examination. The need for this was ex-
pressed when talking about annotating artifacts in assuring quality control. While the
need is somewhat satisfied, the execution of the functionality lacks as the sheet compo-
nent requires at least three clicks per artifact (one click on the ”Report event” button, one
click on the ”Report” button, and one click on the ”X” to close the sheet component). When
more than 50 annotations are made on average during an examination (A.2), it will result
in over 150 clicks. Furthermore, results show that some users would not annotate patient
movements (A.4), which could be a result of it being hidden in a sheet component.
In the expert interviews (A.1), it was mentioned that the neurophysiology assistants will
ask the patient to keep their eyes closed in order to have a less noisy scan. This was
also apparent in the EEG observations (A.2), where the assistant continuously asked the
patient to keep their eyes closed unless asked otherwise. The need for this was also
mentioned by the participants of the focus group (A.3). In the prototype, it is stated on
the ”Relaxation” screens, that it is suggested for the user to let the patient relax with their
eyes closed which seemingly could fulfill this need, but when looking at the results from
the user tests (A.4), it is clear that users forget to tell their patient to have their eyes closed.
Some users even contribute in inevitably worsening the quality by asking the patient to
open their eyes or talking to the patient without making an annotation.
Thus, the user needs, flexible system and quality control, are arguably not fulfilled which
means that the useful section of the User Experience Honeycomb is not satisfied.
4.1.3
Desirable
Desirability is about the design aesthetics and the visual appeal of the application. To
assess whether the prototype fulfills the desirable section of the User Experience Honey-
comb, Schneiderman’s 8 Golden Design Rules were applied.
Strive for consistency
In the 4.1.1 Usable subsection, the consistency and standards usability heuristic was
discussed. Here, it was mentioned how the ”Guided exercises” flow practiced good con-
sistency in the design and functionality of the flow, as well as the consistency of how
the exercises are displayed (see figure 4.3), and the orange completion screen after an
exercise is done (see figure 4.4).
Oppositely, bad consistency is present firstly in the ”Home” screen in the card component
which were at times clickable and at times not (see figure 4.10). During the ”Pre-interview”
flow, the alerts presented to the user had an incorrect usage of semantic colors (see figure
4.6) creating inconsistency in what a user might be used to seeing in other applications.
The inconsistency continues, in the ”Prepare equipment” flow where a ”Next” button had
reallocated from its previous location (see figure 4.11). Furthermore, during the ”Guided
exercises” flow a ”Skip” button was replaced by a ”Finish recording” button (see figure
4.12). Generally, buttons and hyperlinks were used for seemingly the same functionality
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
41
==PAGE:49==
interchangeably (see figure 4.13) which was also the case for the ”X” and ”Cancel” buttons
(see figure 4.14).
Figure 4.21: Different use of colors representing the status connections
Furthermore, the green and yellow colors used for representing the connection of the
GND and REF electrodes as well as the amplifier and the ECG is different than the green
and yellow colors used for representing the connection of the rest of the EEG electrodes
(see figure 4.21).
Therefore, the prototype as a whole does have challenges within consistency, thus it does
not satisfy the strive for consistency design rule.
Enable frequent users to use shortcuts
It is important for an application not only to be easy to learn for a new user, but also
accommodate shortcuts for frequent users to seamlessly navigate the application [7].
The structure of the ”Pre-interview” flow including 13 questions with each question hav-
ing its own screen (see figure 4.22) does increase the ease of learn since the flow is
thoroughly specified, but this flow does not accommodate frequent users. In a scenario
where a user might have five examinations in one day, the amount of screens make the
navigation of the prototype seem long and tedious for the frequent user.
This violates the rule meaning that the enable frequent users to use shortcuts design rule
is not satisfied.
Offer informative feedback
The prototype offers informative feedback for its users throughout. An example of this is
seen in the progress bar in the ”Pre-interview”, ”Prepare Equipment”, ”Guided exercises”,
and ”Evaluation” flows (see figure 4.1 above the connection status button) giving an infor-
42
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:50==
Figure 4.22: ”Pre-interview” flow with individual screens for each question
mative overview of the progress to the user. Furthermore, the alert messages presented
throughout the prototype (see figure 4.6) sufficiently offers understandable feedback to the
user. Lastly, the orange screens in the prototype (see figure 4.4) present the upcoming
exercise or step in the flow which creates a good overview for the user.
A lacking factor, is seen during the ”Prepare Equipment” flow where the user has to con-
nect the amplifier (see figure 4.8). As mentioned in the 4.1.1 Usable subsection, the
lack of informative feedback of the connection to the amplifier causes no visibility in the
system status.
Despite the case of the amplifier connection, the prototype generally satisfies the offer
informative feedback design rule.
Design dialogue to yield closure
Using dialogue to create closure in terms of user actions is important for the sense of
completion for the user [7]. The orange screens throughout the prototype (see figure
4.4) include an encouraging text after the completion of each exercise. Also, the last
orange screen which has the text ”You’re done!” (see figure 4.20) written provides an
accomplishing feeling for the user.
Thus, the design dialogue to yield closure design rule is satisfied.
Offer simple error handling
As mentioned in the 4.1.1 Usable subsection, error handling is included in the prototype
mainly through alert messages and help options (see figure 4.6 and 4.7). Therefore, the
prototype satisfies the offer simple error handling design rule.
Permit easy reversal of actions
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
43
==PAGE:51==
Committing a regrettable action is a commonly seen scenario for a user in a system.
Therefore, permitting the user to easily reverse their actions is crucial [7]. The lack of
back navigation, which was also mentioned in 4.1.1 Usable subsection, greatly violates
this design rule. An example of this was seen in the user tests (A.4), where one user
accidentally skips a step in the flow but has no way of redeeming the error.
Furthermore, the annotation of an artifact offers no option to be removed in case the user
makes an error (see figure 4.16).
Thus, the permit easy reversal of actions design rule is not satisfied.
Support internal locus of control
Permitting the user to be the main trigger for an action causing an application to act in an
expected way is the essence of the support internal locus of control design rule, as an
user would like to feel in control of an application [7].
Overall, the prototype complies to the design rule by respecting the user’s need for control.
However, in the users tests (A.4) it was seen for the application flow, that the exercise timer
started when the user clicked on the ”Go to exercises” button meaning that despite the
user being the actual trigger of the action they did not seem to percieve in that way. As
mentioned in the 4.1.1 Usable section, the users found the instance stressful.
Therefore, the support internal locus design rule is arguably not satisfied.
Reduce short-term memory load
This design rule refers to the limitations of an user’s attention which makes it essential for
the design of the information hierarchy on the user interface to be simple [7].
As mentioned in the 4.1.1 Usable section, one user during the user tests (A.4.3), intu-
itively mistook the given information by asking the patient to relax for three minutes instead
of the intended 10 minutes during the ”Relaxation” flow. Users also mentioned that the
white card, showing the content of the upcoming exercise, would take away their atten-
tion from anything else on the orange screen in the application. This is an example of bad
information hierarchy which means that the reduce short-term memory load design rule
is not satisfied.
All in all, it was seen that while the prototype did satisfy the offer informative feedback,
design dialogue to yield closure, and offer simple error handling design rules, the appli-
cation violated the strive for consistency, enable frequent users to use shortcuts, permit
easy reversal of actions, support internal locus of control, and reduce short-term memory
load design rules to different degrees.
Thus, five out of Shneiderman’s 8 Golden Rules were violated meaning that the desirable
section of the User Experience Honeycomb is not satisfied.
44
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:52==
4.1.4
Findable
The information and navigation of an application should be easily manageable for the user
to find it findable [6].
The prototype has multiple places for the user to find help as mentioned in the 4.1.1
Usable subsection. The results of the user tests (A.4) show that, the users seemed
satisfied with the navigation flow as it was said to be easy, but as mentioned in the 4.1.2
Useful subsection, the enforced linear navigation causes bigger challenges for the user
in practice.
Thus, the findable section of the User Experience Honeycomb is relatively unsatisfied.
4.1.5
Accessible
For an application to be accessible, it must consider users with different disabilities [6]. To
adjudge whether the prototype fulfills the accessible section, the 7 principles of universal
design will be applied.
Equitable use
Having a design with equitable use means, that people with diverse abilities find the design
useful [8]. While many different types of disabilities exist, low vision is one of the most
common [13].
Here, contrast ratio compliance is important to make sure that people with low vision can
utilize the application without difficulties. A contrast checker from WebAIM [14], which
respects the contrast ratio guidelines from Web Content Accessibility Guidelines (WCAG)
[15], was applied on the prototype. The results are presented in table 4.1.
The results in table 4.1 show that out of 21 areas of concern, two areas were compli-
ant while 19 areas were not. Based on these results, the prototype does not satisfy the
equitable use principle.
Flexibility in use
This principle requires that the design widely covers individual preferences of the user [8].
The prototype manages to do so in some sense by having icons on their ”Guided exer-
cises” screens in addition to the exercise texts (see figure 4.3). This then accommodates
users who are dyslexic, and therefore seek guidance in visually displayed information.
Therefore, the prototype relatively satisfies the flexibility in use principle.
Simple and intuitive use
In the user tests (A.4), the application was said to be simple and intuitive. Despite this,
usability did have challenges as mentioned in 4.1.1 Usable subsection. The overall
downfall was the flow where some users would find it confusing and at times not intuitive
(A.4). Thus, the simple and intuitive use principle is arguably not satisfied.

Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:54==
An application should display needed information to accommodate users who have sen-
sory challenges [8].
The prototype’s usage of icons does aid the user’s ability to perceive information which
complies with the principle, but the text heavy sections on some screens (i.e. ”Relaxation”
screens (see figure 4.4)) were proved to cause confusion for the user (A.4.3). Thus, the
perceptible information principle is not satisfied.
Tolerance for error
As the name of the principle states, the design of an application should show tolerance
for possible errors focusing on users with disabilities [8].
In the prototype, alert messages are displayed during different scenarios as mentioned
previously. For example, during the ”Pre-interview” flow, where the user will see alert mes-
sages if they input contradictory patient answers guiding them to consult clinical guide-
lines (see figure 4.6). Thus, the user is prevented from committing an error in terms of
the patient.
Despite these warnings satisfying a part of the principle, the lack of back navigation in
the prototype could result in a point-of-no-return scenario for the user. The weight of this
scenario inevitably results in the tolerance for error principle not being satisfied.
Low physical effort
This principle focuses on having a design which is comfortable to use and does not trigger
excessive fatigue [8]. In the EEG observations (A.2), it was seen that the neurophysiol-
ogy assistant continuously made annotations throughout the examination. This was also
described in the 4.1.2 Useful subsection, where the amount of clicks were tripled as a
result of how the artifact annotation functionality is structured (see figure 4.16). For peo-
ple who suffer from arthritis or are in other ways motor challenged, fatigue occurs when
overworked. This leads to the prototype not satisfying the low physical effort principle.
Size and space for approach and use
Throughout the prototype, the components are presented in a way which seems to fulfill
this principle as the components are well sized and spaced (see figure 4.3). The hyperlink
buttons seen throughout could arguably be bigger but overall it seems that the prototype
satisfies the size and space for approach and use principle.
Conclusively, the prototype violated the equitable use, simple and intuitive use, percep-
tible information, tolerance for error, and low physical effort principles meaning that five
out of the 7 Principles of Universal Design were not fulfilled. Therefore, the accessible
section of the User Experience Honeycomb is not satisfied.
4.1.6
Credible
The Elements of Value Pyramid will be applied to the prototype to determine whether it
fulfills the credible section of the User Experience Honeycomb.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
47
==PAGE:55==
Functional
Starting from the bottom at the functional level, the prototype accomplishes at least three
of the 14 elements. Firstly, it simplifies the examination which is proved by the fact that
nurses with no prior training in EEG or epilepsy would be able to conduct a standard EEG
scan as seen in the results of the user tests (A.4). Furthermore, the system reduces cost
by being cheaper than other products on the current market [1]. Lastly, it manages to
avoid hassles by solving the challenges of the lacking relevant equipment and trained
staff in LMICs as mentioned in the 3.1 Users section.
Emotional
On the emotional level, the wellness element is satisfied since the prototype revolves
around improving health by aiding a patient in their journey of getting a diagnosis to then
receive relevant treatment.
The design/aesthetics element is not accomplished by the prototype based on the findings
in the 4.1.1 Usable subsection, because of the aforementioned shortcomings.
Life changing
The system provides hope by firstly making the examination accessible for patients in
LMICs which secondly, creates an opportunity of decreasing the amount of people with
epilepsy who go undiagnosed thus changing their lives for the better.
Social impact
In terms of social impact, the prototype, as mentioned, helps undiagnosed people, thus
the prototype ultimately reaches the highest level on the Elements of Value Pyramid by
accomplishing the self transcendence element.
Overall, the prototype accomplishes a variety of elements on different levels. While the
design/aesthetics element on the emotional level is arguably lacking, the credible section
of the User Experience Honeycomb is satisfied.
4.1.7
Valuable
Finding a system valuable can be a subjective opinion of every individual user. For a
more objective assessment, whether the prototype fulfills the valuable section of the User
Experience Honeycomb will be adjudged by an accumulation of the findings presented in
the analysis.
Throughout the analysis, the different sections of the User Experience Honeycomb have
been assessed. Starting with the usable section, the prototype had challenges within six
of the 10 usability heuristics resulting in the usable section not being satisfied. The use-
ful section focused on two user needs, flexible system and quality control, which were
respected to some degree but different findings in the expert interviews (A.1), EEG ob-
servations (A.2), and the user tests (A.4) showed challenges leading the useful section
to not be satisfied. Based on Schneidermann’s 8 Golden Design Rules, it was found that
48
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:56==
the prototype did not satisfy the desirable section of the User Experience Honeycomb.
Through the 7 Principles of Universal Design, the prototype was examined on different
perspectives concerning accessibility. This examination, showed that the prototype vio-
lated a majority of the principles concluding that the accessible section was not satisfied.
The only satisfied section of the User Experience Honeycomb is the credible section.
Here, the prototype successfully accomplished different elements across the levels of the
Elements of Value Pyramid.
All in all, a majority of sections of the User Experience Honeycomb are not fulfilled mean-
ing that the valuable section is not satisfied.
4.2
SWOT Analysis
The prototype was adjudged in the 4.1 User Experience section where both usability
strengths and weaknesses from different aspects were discovered. Now, a SWOT analy-
sis will be used to evaluate the possible strengths, weaknesses, opportunities, and threats
of the whole system, including the application (V1) and equipment, from both internal and
external factors. The main points discussed are:
Strengths
Weaknesses
Application based system
Quality control
BrainCapture advantages
System limitations
Opportunities
Threats
User outcomes
Dependant variables
Expansion possibilities
Holistic aspects
4.2.1
Strengths & Weaknesses
The BrainCapture system is centered around the application and is therefore an applica-
tion based system which has its benefits. Firstly, an application is more compact com-
pared to a typical EEG system which includes heavy machinery. The compact aspect
also makes it easier to transport the system to conduct examinations outside of a hospi-
tal setting. Also, a benefit of using an application rather than a specified machine lies in
the familiarity many have with an application. System users might feel more comfortable
dealing with an application based system since they are used to dealing with applications
in their everyday life.
With the application centric system, BrainCapture has managed to enable nurses with no
background or prior education in EEG or epilepsy to conduct a quality EEG scan proving
that applying an application based system on this domain is possible with a high usability
and learnability compared to the typical EEG system. This is done by giving the user a
crash course including introduction and simulation training as well as the user guidance
in the application given throughout the examination.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
49
==PAGE:57==
Lastly, an advantage for BrainCapture is in terms of regulations, they currently have ac-
quired medical approval for their system. This strengthens the system compared to a
competing system, as customers and users can be assured that the system is safely
applicable on patients.
On the flip side, the system also comes with its weaknesses. The quality control, which
was proved to be a user need in the 3.2 User needs section, might be worse than the
quality produced by a typical EEG system because of missing features and functionalities.
In typical EEG systems, the neurophysiology assistant can see the EEG waves live while
also monitoring the patient through a synchronized video which is saved as a part of the
examination and be used for reviewing later on in the process. The lack of these features
result in a higher need for artifact annotations from the user, but still it is doubtful that the
quality of the examination will be on the same level. Another limitation of the system is in
the types of EEG scans possible to conduct. While it is possible to do a standard EEG as
well as a sleep EEG, the system might not be appropriate for video or EMU EEG scans
as these have more requirements for the system.
Other weaknesses are seen in regards to system limitations. Firstly, the application has
local storage limitations meaning that the user will be able to save a limited amount of
examinations locally but once the storage limit is reached, the user will have to upload the
examination to the cloud and delete the local files. Secondly, the accessibility compliancy
was not fulfilled, as discussed in 4.1.5 Accessible subsection, resulting in some direct
users with possible disabilities not being able to utilize the system.
4.2.2
Opportunities & Threats
One of the main opportunities brought by the BrainCapture system is the outcomes it will
bring users. The network created between direct and indirect users across borders allows
neurologists in HICs to connect with nurses in LMICs, thus reaching people with limited
resources. This is also due to the affordability of the system.
The system also opens up for expansion possibilities. For example, the mobility of the
system will make it possible for the user to conduct scans in the homes of the patients.
Overall, the system is adaptable for different use cases including it being used for other
research purposes and illnesses beyond epilepsy.
Contrarily, dependant variables and holistic aspects could threaten the system.
In terms of dependant variables, it is meant for the system to be connected to WiFi, but
the circumstances of LMICs could challenge this element. In a scenario where a nurse
has travelled to a village where no WiFi is accessible, the nurse does have the option of
conducting offline scans, but the aforementioned local storage limitations come into play,
because if the nurse has to conduct an excessive amount of scans, the limitations will be
a threat. It is also meant for the nurses to receive a crash course as mentioned before, but
if a scenario arises where the clinical facility does not have the resources to provide such
courses, it is not certain that the nurse will be able to conduct the examination satisfactorily.
50
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:58==
Furthermore, the aforementioned missing features could result in customers choosing a
more inclusive system from a competitor on the market.
Threats are also seen from holistic aspects. The political and economic state of the LMIC
might also threaten the system. If political or economic challenges arise in the countries
of the customers, they might be forced to renege despite prior established relationships
with the company. Furthermore, the company currently has a relationship with Kenya as
a beachhead market, but are still seeking market validation from other countries meaning
that the future of the system is still unclear.
The fact that the company has its market in LMICs mean that import and export between
Europe and Africa is needed. Here, complications could occur which could threaten the
livelihood of the system. The import and export challenges could mirror in regards to the
externally purchased equipment. At the moment, the company purchases their cap and
the hardware for their amplifier at external European suppliers, and while the company
seems confident in its external suppliers, situations where complications could challenge
the relationship could arise prompting another threat.
Other holistic aspects include legislation or other types of regulations which could threaten
the BrainCapture system. As mentioned, the system is currently not accessibility compli-
ant, as seen in the 4.1.5 Accessible subsection, which not only causes an inconve-
nience for users with disabilities but in some countries such as Denmark, America, and
South Africa, accessibility regulations are in place [16, 17, 18]. Thus, the system’s inability
to comply with accessibility regulations could result in legal threats to the system if similar
regulations are made in the intended markets.
4.3
Sub-conclusion
In the 4.1 User Experience Review section, it was seen that the prototype, which the
BrainCapture application is based on, has both strengths and weaknesses in terms of
usability, accessibility, and more. Overall, the weaknesses outweighed the strengths.
Furthermore, it was discussed in the 4.2 SWOT Analysis section that the system has
strengths by beneficially being an application based system and having advantages over
competitors by being medically approved, but weaknesses of the system included lack in
features affecting quality control and system limitations. The system also produced op-
portunities creating user outcomes and expansion possibilities, however multiple threats
could occur as a result of dependant variables and holistic aspects.
Conclusively, the state of the user interface and the user experience is lacking, thus the
development of a data-driven optimized version of the BrainCapture solution is justified.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
51
==PAGE:59==
5
Design Implementation & Testing
The 5 Design Implementation & Testing chapter focuses on answering the third re-
search question by looking at how an optimized version of the BrainCapture solution would
look in terms of user interface and user experience design. This will be done through three
sections.
The first section of the chapter focuses on a MoSCoW Prioritization where must have,
should have, could have, and will not have will be used to filter which of the functionali-
ties discovered in the previous chapter would be included in an optimized version of the
solution.
Note that the optimized version of the solution is at an MVP level, thus it will not be able
to cover all the weaknesses of the BrainCapture prototype which were stated in the 4
Analysis chapter.
Once the functionalities have been filtered, an user story map centered around the user,
their goals, activities, and tasks as well as system tasks is constructed in the second
section of the chapter.
In the third section of the chapter, the design implementation begins based on the two
first sections. The implementation and testing will happen across five sprints which follow
the build-measure-learn cycle. The testing will be conducted on relevant users including
nurses, medical laboratory technologists, and social and health care assistants who have
the same tasks as a nurse. For each sprint, improvements will be made to the application
based on the test results.
Finally, an optimized version of user interface and user experience design developed
through a data-driven approach is the end result.
5.1
MoSCoW Prioritization
Direct and indirect users and their needs were examined through expert interviews and
EEG observations in the 3 Research chapter, followed by an extensive analysis where
elements such as usability, desirability, accessibility, and more, were discussed in an ex-
pert user experience review, and different risk related factors were presented through a
SWOT analysis in the 4 Analysis chapter.
This has led to the discovery of different functionalities. Table 5.1 shows an overview of
all the functionalities.
These functionalities will be filtered through a MoSCoW Prioritization compromised of
must have, should have, could have, and will not have functionalities.

53
==PAGE:61==
The final optimized MVP version of the application will only include the must have and
should have functionalities. The functionalities for could have and will not have could be
included in a later developed version of the application which is then not a part of the MVP,
and therefore beyond the scope of this thesis.
5.1.1
Must have
It was discovered through the expert interviews (A.1), and more specifically in the EEG
observations (A.2), that the patient will have to wear the equipment before the exam-
ination can be conducted. The equipment is therefore a must in order for the user to
conduct the examination. Furthermore, it was discovered that the system would have to
be flexible and respect free navigation in order to best accommodate the user and patient.
Instances have shown that system flexibility is exploited repeatedly in practice making it
a highly necessary functionality. It was clear from the 3.2 User Needs section that an
annotation functionality was a key need in order to contribute to the quality of an EEG
scan. In the 4.2 SWOT analysis section, it was also expressed that the lack of other
features, i.e. video and live EEG, increased the need for an easily accessible annota-
tion functionality making it a must have for the application. Lastly, in the same section, it
was also discussed that local storage limitations could cause a threat which makes the
possibility to upload the examination to the cloud a high need. Lastly, it was explained in
the 3.2 User Needs section, that the neurophysiology assistants followed patient related
restrictions influenced by their clinical guidelines. For a nurse who is untrained in EEG
and epilepsy, the restrictions are a high necessity for patient security.
Therefore, functionality numbers 2, 4, 7, and 11 are to be included in the MVP application.
5.1.2
Should have
It is possible to conduct a standard EEG scan without knowing exactly who the patient
is. This is seen in scenarios where neurophysiology assistants have to conduct emer-
gency EEG scans where they do not necessarily know anything about the patient until
after the examination. Nevertheless, the functionality of inputting patient information in-
cluding routine questions brings immense value and is a part of the standard procedure
as discovered in 3.2 User Needs section. The same scenario can be applied when dis-
cussing the priority of the provocation exercises. Here, it is also important to make the
exercises as easy as possible to follow for an inexperienced nurse. Another part of the
standard procedure is the review of the scan. Here, the preliminary evaluation of the scan
contributes to the overall analysis and diagnosis meaning that the functionality brings high
value.
Thus, the functionality numbers 1, 5, and 10 will also be included in the MVP application.
5.1.3
Could have
In the 3.2 User Needs section, the need for synchronized video was deemed highly
important from the perspectives of the neurophysiology assistants, medical laboratory
technologist, and the specialized neurology and neurophysiology doctor who use it as
a tool for diagnostic purposes and quality control. The prioritization of this functionality
54
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:62==
is affected by the limited resources of LMICs. An external camera is not a part of the
BrainCapture solution. Utilizing the camera on the phone, which is a part of the solution,
will result in practical issues such as the nurse having to simultaneously film the patient
while manipulating the application, as well as the size of the examination file including a
video. Despite bringing value, this feature is therefore prioritized as a could have feature.
In regards to the file size, the possibility of automatically deleting files locally after being
uploaded to the cloud could counterattack the aforementioned threat. Despite this, the
possibility will need more user research before being enforced as the feature has currently
not been tested in practice. The need for further user research makes this a could have
feature as well.
This results in the functionality numbers 6 and 12 not being included in the MVP applica-
tion.
5.1.4
Will not have
One neurophysiology assistant (A.1.1) mentioned that they would ask for the patient’s
signature while other neurophysiology assistants at another hospital did not mention this.
Therefore, the inclusion of a patient signature feature is not crucial. In the 3.2 User Needs
section, it was mentioned that there is a change in montages during the examination
which is done manually by the neurophysiology assistant. While this seems to be a stan-
dard procedure, the level of knowledge and training required for this specific action might
be too complex for a nurse without any background in EEG and epilepsy. Thus, the ap-
plication will not have this feature.
Furthermore, challenges in the shape of patient behavior were discovered in the 3.2 User
Needs section. Here, it was mentioned that knowing how to react if a patient has a
seizure during the examination could be difficult. To accommodate this challenge, includ-
ing seizure handling or other guidance in the application could be an option. However,
different hospitals have different ways of handling a seizure attack, thus standardized
guidelines could prove to be difficult to define. In a scenario where a seizure does hap-
pen and the nurse follows the guidance of the application, which might be conflicting to
the hospital’s, it could have a critical outcome for the patient. Thus, this feature will be ex-
cluded. Lastly, the possibility of conducting a control EEG was mentioned in the 3.2 User
Needs section. This feature is outside of the scope, and will therefore not be included in
the application.
Thus, the functionality numbers 3, 8, 9, and 13 will not be included in the MVP application.
Table 5.1 can now be updated after the prioritization of the functionalities as seen in table
5.2.
5.2
User Story Map
Now, that the needed functionalities have been filtered, a user story map focusing on
the user’s goals, activities, and tasks can be created. The must have and should have

Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:64==
functionalities will be a part of slice one which is the MVP, the could have functionalities
will be a part of slice two, and the will not have functionalities (except for the seizure
handling/guidance function which is excluded for being too risky) will be in slice three.
Slice two and three are outside of the MVP scope, and will therefore not be described
further.
Firstly, the user, who is a nurse in a LMIC, has three goals which is compromised of
different activities with respective user and system tasks. The goals are to 1) conduct, 2)
evaluate, and 3) complete a quality standard EEG scan.
The first goal, of conducting a quality standard EEG scan, has 10 activities which follow
the standard flow of an EEG examination. First, to prepare for the scan the tasks are
to prepare relevant equipment (phone with application, cap with EEG electrodes, gel,
syringe with needle, amplifier, and ECG device), and then to turn on the app. Then,
the user gets relevant patient information by asking the patient about their identification
number, biological sex, birthday, current state, episode activity, and medical history. If the
patient falls into either age or medically related restrictions, the system should show a
warning to notify the user. Once the patient information is received, the user should save
the information, and the system should save the progress. Hereafter, the user will connect
the EEG electrodes by putting the cap on the patient, turning on the amplifier, connecting
the app to the amplifier via Bluetooth, which the system reflects, then connecting the
cap to the amplifier via the wire, gelling up the EEG electrodes on the cap, and lastly,
checking the impedance status of the electrodes. Subsequently, the user connects the
ECG electrodes by putting on the ECG on the patient, connecting the ECG to the amplifier
via the wire, and once again checking the impedance status.
Thus, the user can start the scan by starting the recording which triggers the system to
start the recording time. Next, the user can do the exercises, eyes, hyperventilation, and
photic stimulation, by asking the patient to perform the exercises where the user will add
annotations throughout. The user can then finish the exercises and the system will save
the progress.
Throughout the exercises, the system will notify the user if seizure like behaviour is de-
tected, and the user has the option of skipping exercises if necessary. Also, it will notify
the user if the patient is falling asleep, and if electrodes loose their connection.
Afterwards, the user should let the patient rest for the remaining examination time where
the user has the same possibilities as with the exercises, and the system responds in the
same way as well. Lastly for the goal of conducting a quality standard EEG scan, the user
can finish the scan by taking the equipment off the patient when the time is up, and then
turning off the amplifier.
The second goal, of evaluating a quality standard EEG scan, has one activity which is to
review the scan. The user can do this by noting how the scan went, edit annotations if
necessary, and note final comments. The system will then save the progress.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
57
==PAGE:65==
Lastly, the third goal, of completing a quality standard EEG scan, also has one activity
which is to upload the reviewed EEG scan. Here, the user will have to save the file and
upload it to the cloud which the system then does.
A visual representation of the user story map can be seen in (A.7.1).
5.3
Sprints
With the construction of the user story map, the design implementation, with the aim of
testing on relevant users, can begin.
The design implementation and testing will cover five sprints, which follow the build-
measure-learn cycle iteratively.
The first sprint focuses on the initial version of the prototype (V0) focusing on color choice
and illustrations for the user interface design, and the included prioritized functionalities
in regards to the user experience design. The prototype will be reviewed by an external
user experience designer, and a medical student at the University of Copenhagen, and
will thereafter be updated accordingly.
For the second sprint, an updated version of the prototype (V1) will be tested on four
nurses who participated in the user tests of BrainCapture’s application (A.4). The proto-
type is then updated and the cycle continuous.
For the third and fourth sprints, different versions of the prototype (V2 for the third sprint
and V3 for the fourth) will be tested, and for each respective sprint, four nurses with no
prior knowledge of EEG or epilepsy will be the test users (A.5.2, A.5.3). Based on the test
results, improvements will be implemented.
In the final sprint, the same cycle is continued but after the testing with four nurses is
completed (A.5.4), final comments on the results of the last test round will be discussed,
but no improvements will be implemented on the prototype (V4).
In total, this will accumulate to two feedback sessions (one with the user experience de-
signer, and a second with the medical student), and four rounds of testing with 16 test
users (four in each sprint) (A.5). Thus, the final end result will be an MVP prototype which
has been optimized through a data-driven process.
Note that throughout the sprints, the focus will be on the impact of the improvements and
whether they have solved the challenges, as well as new challenges if any occur. Other
areas which are consistently successful for the users will not be described further.
Test setup
During the testing rounds (sprint 2-5), two types of data were collected, namely quantita-
tive and qualitative data.
For the quantitative data collection, four test cases were measured with different user ex-
perience goals, measures, measurement instruments, metric, baseline levels, and target

Table 5.3: Test cases
levels (motivated by Table 16-1 in [3]). These can be seen in table 5.3.
For all the test cases, the user class, as stated, is a nurse with no prior experience in
working with epilepsy or EEG. The age and gender of the nurse is not considered as it
does not have a direct impact on the test results. In some cases, where nurses are not
available for testing, social and health care assistants or medical laboratory technologists
can also be utilized, if they have the same tasks and function at a hospital as a nurse.
The target level is to be equal or better than the baseline level. For each sprint, the results
of the previous sprint will be the baseline level meaning that the level changes every sprint.
The first test case, seen in the first row in table 5.3 has the goal ”Low error rates”, and to
accomplish this, the error performance of the user conducting three tasks will be measured
by counting the error rate. The three tasks are:
1. Pre scan
• Input patient information
• Connect and put on equipment
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
59
==PAGE:67==
2. During scan
• Annotate eyes open, eyes closed and blink continuously
• Annotate using “other”
• Remove annotation
3. Post scan
• Edit an annotation
• Review scan
• Upload scan to cloud
The baseline level for this test case is based on the average number of errors made by
the users in the user test on the BrainCapture application (A.4), where the same tasks
were conducted in the application.
The second test case’s goal is ”User attraction to product” where the user’s opinion on
attractiveness will be measured using a questionnaire scale which ranges from 1-5 where
1 is the worst and 5 is the best. The baseline level is established by general recommen-
dations and guidelines stating that for a score to be above average, it should be around
70% [19].
The third test case has the goal ”Quality of user experience” which will be measuring the
user’s opinion of overall experience.
The last test case focuses on ”App guidance”, and measures the user’s confidence to
conduct a scan if they were to do it again tomorrow.
Both of these are measured on the same 1-5 questionnaire scale, and the baseline level
is based on the ratings of the users from the user tests on the BrainCapture application
(A.4).
For the qualitative data collection, the users were presented with equipment (EEG cap,
amplifier, ECG device, syringe, gel, and measuring tape) and a patient (mannequin head)
as seen in figure 5.1.
The users were then asked to start the application and simulate a scan on the patient.
For each round, a think aloud test was conducted where the users were asked to vocalize
their thoughts and possible challenges.
5.3.1
First sprint
User Interface Design
Simplicity is the main theme behind the design of the user interface. The main elements
of the user interface is the use of color and illustrations & icons.
Color
60
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:68==
Figure 5.1: Usability test setup
The color blue symbolizes loyalty and trust [20], and some of the biggest medical compa-
nies in the world, including Novarts [21], Pfizer [22], Roche [23], Regeneron [24], Abbvie
[25], and Novo Nordisk [26], unanimously use different shades of blue as their main color
throughout their branding, services, and more. BrainCapture advertise themselves as a
company working with medical technology [27], and it therefore makes them eligible to
also use blue in their services.
Figure 5.2: The selected ”French Blue” style color
The choice of blue was made using the aforementioned contrast checker from WebAIM
[14], where different shades and nuances of blue were assessed in terms of accessibility
against an off white background. This background was chosen to keep a simplistic and
consistent design, and to assure that the background did not take away attention from
any potential important information or components. A ”French Blue” style color, which
respects the contrast ratio guidelines, was finally chosen (see figure 5.2).
Furthermore, accessible pink and grey colors were chosen alongside the blue to represent
the cap sizes which are similar to the colors of the caps (see figure 5.3). The pink cap is
a small, the blue is a medium while the grey is a large.
Lastly, alert messages will be presented throughout the application. The alert messages
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
61
==PAGE:69==
Figure 5.3: Color coordinated cap sizes
Figure 5.4: Semantic color scheme for alert messages
62
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:70==
follow the semantic color scheme in the way that they are orange when the alert has
information the user needs to be attentive of, green when the information is positive, and
red when the alert has information which the user has to quickly act upon. Furthermore,
an icon is present in the alert corresponding to the message (see figure 5.4).
The colors scheme is familiar to most users, and they therefore trigger a reaction before
the user has read the included text.
Furthermore, the semantic colors are also used throughout the application for red delete
buttons, green check marks, and green exercise completions.
Illustrations & icons
Users in the user tests on the BrainCapture application (A.4) responded well to the icons
as many used them as guidance during the examination. Additionally, the users men-
tioned that the inclusion of animations would help with patient interaction and possible
language barriers.
Figure 5.5: A selection of equipment animations
Therefore, it was decided to create and include more explicit animated illustrations in the
new application. These animated illustrations would be present during both the ”Equip-
ment flow”, to show users how the equipment should be connected (see figure 5.5), and
the ”Exercise flow”, which would mirror how the patient should conduct the exercise (i.e.
breathing tempo for hyperventilation, and blinking frequency for photic stimulation) (see
figure 5.6).
Furthermore, icons will have a big impact on the annotation component. Here, every
single annotation will have its own respective icon which will create visual association for
the user (see figure 5.7).
User Experience Design

==PAGE:71==

Figure 5.7: Annotation component

Data-driven optimization of UI & UX

The user experience design of the new application will focus on usability by being easy
to use for first time users, as well as accommodating frequent users by being efficient
in use. The user experience design of each flow, covering the prioritized functionalities
mentioned in table 5.2, will be described below.
Home
Figure 5.8: Home screen
The Home screen of the application includes two buttons; One that says ”Start scan”,
which starts a new scan taking the user to the ”Patient flow”, and another which says
”Review scan”, directing the user to the ”Review EEG flow” where they can evaluate an
existing scan (see figure 5.8). Both buttons have big illustrations which help the user to
visually distinguish between the two.
Patient information flow
The ”Patient Information flow” is divided in four main steps with input fields and dropdown
menus including a small title above each representing a question (see figure 5.9). The
questions, which are motivated by the findings in the 3 Research chapter, will be placed
inside the fields in the shape of help text. Within each step, there is a page count to
keep the user informed of their progress in the flow. For all the steps, the user always
has the option of skipping by clicking a ”Skip” button on the top right corner, and going
back in the flow by clicking a back chevron button on the top left corner. Also, a ”Next”
button is present throughout the flow which is always active to further respect the need
of a flexible system. This is in case the user wants to only answer some of the questions
due to different practical circumstances.
Dividing the ”Patient Information flow” in these four main steps will offer a more compact
and efficient way of inputting the information that does not overwhelm the user.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
65
==PAGE:73==
Figure 5.9: Patient Information flow
The steps are Patient, Current state, Episodes, and Medical history.
The Patient step asks the questions:
• What is the patient’s personal identification?
• What is the patient’s date of birth?
• What is the sex of the patient?
Once the user has answered the questions in the Patient step, they can click a ”Next”
button at the bottom of the screen taking them to the Current state step, which asks the
questions:
• Is the patient in their habitual state?
• Is the patient well rested?
• Is the patient sleep deprived?
• Is the patient pregnant?
• Has the patient ate and drunk?
• When was the last meal the patient had?
Again, the user clicks a ”Next” button at the bottom of the page after finishing, taking them
to the Episodes step, which asks the questions:
• When did the patient last have an episode?
• What is the episode frequency of the patient?
66
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:74==
Another click on a ”Next” button takes the user to the Medical history step, which asks the
questions:
• Does the patient have diagnosed heart diseases or arrhythmia?
• Does the patient have diagnosed high blood pressure?
• Has the patient had cerebral hemorrhage within the last 2 years?
• Has the patient had a blood clot in brain or heart within the last 2 years?
• Has the patient had a heart surgery within the last 2 years?
• Does the patient have diagnosed lung diseases, asthma or COPD?
• Is the patient on any medication?
After the user has completed the last step, the ”Next” button has been changed to a ”Go
to equipment” button, which takes them to the next flow.
Figure 5.10: Patient restriction alerts
During the inputting of patient information in the ”Patient Information flow”, different alert
messages can be triggered depending on the inputted data (see figure 5.10). If the pa-
tient is above 50 years old, the alert message ”It is not suitable for the patient to do the
hyperventilation exercise” will be shown, and likewise the message ”It is not suitable for
the patient to do the photic stimulation exercise” is shown if the patient is below one year.
A pregnant patient will trigger the message ”It is not suitable for the patient to do the
provocation exercises”, and a patient with diagnosed lung diseases, asthma or COPD will
lead to the message ”It is not suitable for the patient to do the hyperventilation exercise.”
The alert messages will be presented in an accessible red making the user aware of a
patient related restriction without dictating how to act upon this restriction.

==PAGE:75==
Equipment flow
The ”Equipment flow” guides the user on how to deal with the equipment and equip the
patient. This is done through a series of screens with animated illustrations and corre-
sponding text. The user navigates through the flow using a ”Next” button at the bottom of
the screen.
Figure 5.11: List of equipment
The first screen presented to the user shows them a list of all the equipment they will need
to conduct the scan, to ensure they have all the needed equipment (see figure 5.11). The
list includes one EEG cap, one amplifier, one ECG device (wires and sticker electrodes),
conductive salt-water gel, one syringe, and a measuring tape. When ready, the user can
click a ”Connect equipment” button.
The user is asked to measure the head of the patient using the measuring tape (see figure
5.12). On this screen the user also sees the aforementioned three color coordinated
standard cap sizes which are available as a part of the BrainCapture solution. This will
help the user in knowing which cap size to choose based on the measurement.
Next, the patient is asked to put the EEG cap on the patient’s head whereafter the user is
asked to turn on the amplifier by sliding the knob on the side (see figure 5.13).
Then, they are asked to connect it to the application via Bluetooth. This is done by choos-
ing the correct amplifier in the dropdown menu and waiting until the connection is complete
which is represented by a green check mark (see figure 5.14). This is the only screen
where the ”Next” button starts in an inactive state, and once the connection is completed,
the button changes to an active state.
The user is then asked to connect the cap to the amplifier via the wire (see figure 5.15).
Hereafter, the user is asked to take the syringe and fill it with gel (see figure 5.15).

Exercises flow
The first screen the user sees in the ”Exercise flow”, is a list of all the exercises (see
figure 5.18). To guide the user, the exercises are listed to follow the order of the standard
procedure starting with the eyes, then hyperventilation, photic stimulation, and lastly a
resting exercise. But the user is allowed to choose whichever order they see fit meaning
that they can choose to start with the photic stimulation exercise followed by the eyes
exercise if desired. Within each list item, an illustration of the exercise is present, along
with the estimated duration, to give the user a visual representation of the exercise. At
the bottom of the screen, an inactive ”Save” button is present. In order for the button to
become active, at least one exercise has to be completed.
When the user clicks the desired exercise, they are taken to a screen which has exercise
information text explaining how the user should conduct the exercise (see figure 5.19).
The user can use this text to explain the exercise to the patient, to help themselves, or
however they wish. On the bottom of the screen, a ”Tip” stating ”Tip: Ask the patient to
keep their eyes closed and open their jaw slightly unless asked to do otherwise for best

possible EEG results” is present to increase the quality of examination.
Figure 5.20: Exercise flow
Once the user is ready, they can click a ”Tap to start” button at the top of the screen which
starts a timer counting down until the the end of each exercise part has been completed
(see figure 5.20). The duration stated on the timer for the different steps in the exercise
is based on the findings in the expert interviews (A.1) and EEG observations (A.2).
Furthermore, the aforementioned annotation component is present on all the exercise
screens allowing the user to input annotations throughout the scan (see figure 5.7). The
component consists of six buttons with corresponding icons and titles.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
73
==PAGE:81==
The top row of buttons are for annotating each time the patient opens, closes, or blinks
their eyes when the user asks for it. This is inspired by the EEG observations (A.2) where
the neurophysiology assistant would annotate every time they asked the patient to open,
close, or blink their eyes. The buttons can also be used for deviations in terms of eye
movement.
The bottom row of buttons are only for annotating deviations. Here, talking and seizure
have been selected as predefined annotation options.
Talking was added because in the EEG observation with the 17 year old male (A.2.3), the
neurophysiology assistant annotated the patient talking using a free text field. Therefore,
talking was added as a predefined option for it to be more easy and efficient to annotate.
While it was mentioned in the 3.2 User Needs section that seizures did not to happen
often, the option should still be easily accessible for the user in case it does occur.
Figure 5.21: Adding an ”Other” annotation
Lastly, an ”Other” option is present for when the user wants to annotate something that
differs from the predefined options (see figure 5.21). When the user clicks the ”Other”
button, it will open a sheet component which consists of a large free text field where the
user can freely write the annotation, and save by clicking the check mark at the bottom.
Every time the user adds an annotation, a counter, which is located at the top of the
annotation component, counts the added annotations giving the user an overview of their
annotations. While the main functionality of the counter is to count the added annotations,
the purpose of it is to make sure the user knows the clicked annotation has been added
as a form of user feedback. In practice, it could occur that users click an annotation but
are unaware of it because of distractions or mere human errors.
The composition of the six annotation buttons make it possible to annotate with only one
74
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:82==
Figure 5.22: Removing an annotation
click. However, the easily accessible buttons might increase the error rate by creating
false positive annotations. Therefore, the counter also has a delete functionality where
the interactivity is presented through a chevron icon (see figure 5.22). If the user clicks
the counter, a sheet component opens, which has a list of all the added annotations. To
the right of each annotation, a delete button is present where the user can click to delete
the annotation.
Throughout each step of the exercise, a ”Skip” button is always present at the top right
corner which takes the user one step forward to the next part of the same exercise. Back
navigation is also present through a back chevron located at the top left corner of the
screen which takes the user one step back to the previous part of the exercise.
Furthermore, there is an inactive ”Next” button which turns active once the exercise part
is finished. Similarly, on the final exercise step, a ”Finish” button is present which the user
can click after the final completion (see figure 5.23).
When an exercise is complete, it takes the user back to the screen with the list of exercises,
where the completed exercise has changed color from blue to green (see figure 5.24).
While it is not mandatory to complete all the exercises, the color change helps give the
user an overview of their progress in the examination.
Once the user has completed at least one exercise, they can click the ”Save” button at
the bottom of the screen which then shows a big green check mark expressing that the
scan has been saved by the system, and then takes them back to the Home screen (see
figure 5.24).
During the ”Exercise flow”, four alert messages can be triggered at different times (see
figure 5.24).

==PAGE:83==

Figure 5.24: Completion of first exercise and saving the scan

Data-driven optimization of UI & UX

Figure 5.25: Alert messages
When an electrode has lost connection, the user gets the alert message ”Electrode x has
lost connection!” where x is the name of the specific electrode. The alert message is
orange, and has an exclamation mark in a triangle as an icon, since it needs the user to
pay attention and fix the electrode. Once the electrode is fixed, the alert changes to green,
the text now says ”Electrode x has regained connection”, and the icon also changes to a
check mark in a square.
If the system registers that the patient is about to fall asleep, an alert message is triggered
with the message ”The patient is falling asleep. Wake them up gently.” As mentioned in
the 3 Research chapter, drowsiness creates noisy brainwaves, and the patient therefore
needs to be awake during the exercises (except for the resting exercise). Thus, the user
is alerted with an orange color and the same icon with the exclamation mark in a triangle.
Lastly, the only red alert is triggered when the system detects seizure like behavior, and it
states ”Seizure behavior is detected! Tend to the patient!” This alert is red since it requires
a quick reaction. The text is meant to make the user aware of the behavior to hopefully
stop the seizure before it happens, but the text does not tell the user how to deal with
the seizure as this could be too risky as mentioned in the 5.1 MoSCoW Prioritization
section. The alert also has the exclamation mark in a triangle as its icon.
Review and upload EEG scan
After the completion of a scan, the user can click the ”Review scan” button on the Home
screen (see figure 5.8). This takes the user to a screen with a list of patient scans divided
in two sections; One section at the top called ”To review”, and one at the bottom called
”Reviewed” (see figure 5.26).
”To review” includes all the scans the user has conducted which need to be reviewed.

duration of the scan, and the date the scan was conducted.
”Reviewed” on the other hand includes all the scans which the user has conducted, eval-
uated, and uploaded to the cloud.
When the user clicks on a patient scan in the list, they are taken to a screen with a list
of evaluation questions (see figure 5.27) where the construction is similar to the list of
questions in the ”Patient information flow” with input fields. The evaluation questions are:
• What was the state of the patient during recording?
• What was the quality of the eyes exercise?
• What was the quality of the hyperventilation exercise?
• What was the quality of the photic stimulation exercise?
• Did any episodes occur during the scan?
• Other notes?
At the top of the screen, an edit functionality is seen presented with the overall number
of annotations and a pencil icon (see figure 5.28). The user can click the pencil icon and
see a list of the added annotations through the scan. The user can then click on a specific
annotation to add a comment if needed. The user can then use the back chevron on the
top left corner of the screen to get back to the previous screen with the two sections.
Once the scan has been evaluated by the user through them answering all the questions,
they can click the ”Upload to cloud” button at the bottom of the screen which has turned
from an inactive to an active state. This will trigger a green check mark which confirms

Feedback
The initial version of the prototype (V0), was presented to both an external user experience
designer and a medical student at the University of Copenhagen. Relevant constructive
feedback was given upon this application presentation.
User experience designer
The user experience designer firstly had comments about the color usage mentioning that
the prototype only using one color violated the color hierarchy. Thus, the prioritization of
components or important information based on color usage would be redundant. The
designer also preached to have a more modern design by advocated against the overly
rounded edges.
Lastly, the designer was not adherent at the usage of drop down menus for the questions
in the ”Patient information flow” which had yes or no answers. Furthermore, the designer
disliked that the questions were present in the shape of help text, since the question would
disappear once the user had answered it. They stated, that this could create confusion
when looking at the overview of the answered questions for each page.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
81
==PAGE:89==
Medical student
While the medical student was overall impressed with the prototype and the usability of it,
they had feedback on the illustrations used in the ”Equipment flow” and ”Exercise flow”.
Here, the student stated that the illustrations looked too unprofessional compared to cur-
rent medical systems they were familiar with. They therefore suggested to update the
illustrations so that they looked more like people and less like cartoon figures.
Improvements
Based on the feedback from the user experience designer and the medical student, dif-
ferent improvements were made to the application.
Figure 5.31: Updated color palette
First, the chosen blue became a call-to-action color which was reserved for buttons be-
cause of its vibrant style. Then, a darker shade of blue was chosen to be a primary color
to be used for interactive components which were not buttons i.e. the annotation compo-
nent. Lastly, an even darker shade of blue was chosen as a secondary color which was
for texts, animations, and other non-interactive elements. Thus, a blue monochromatic
design with a color hierarchy was created where each color has its own impact on the
overall user interface design (see figure 5.31).
Furthermore, the round edges were decreased so that the application had a sharper look
creating a more modern design (see figure 5.32).
For the yes or no questions in the ”Patient information flow”, the drop down menus were
replaced by checkboxes (see figure 5.33). For each question, which has been changed
from being help text to normal text, a checkbox was present to the right of the question.
The user could then click the checkbox if the patient answered yes, or not click anything
if the patient answered no. For all the questions which had a yes as an answer, a green
82
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:90==
Figure 5.32: Less rounded edges on components
Figure 5.33: Updated ”Patient information flow” with check boxes
