5.3.2
Second sprint
Test users
The four test users for the second sprint (A.5.1), were recruited after they participated in
the aforementioned user test on the BrainCapture application conducted by the usability
expert (A.4). Therefore, the users will be refereed to as biased users because of their
previous expore to the system.
The first biased test user has 31 years of experience as a nurse in intensive care. The
second biased test user has 37 years of experience as a nurse. The third biased test user
has 11 years of experience as a nurse, and lastly, the fourth biased test user has 17 years
of experience as a nurse.
Test results
Quantitative
On average, the four biased users made five errors. The first biased user had six errors,
the second biased user had seven errors, the third biased user had four errors, and the
last biased user had three errors. Thus, the target level in table 5.3 for the first test case
about ”Low error rates” which was at four errors, has not been reached.
84
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:92==
For the second test case, about ”User attraction to product”, the first and the fourth biased
user rated 4.5/5, the second biased user rated 4/5, and the third biased user rated 3.5/5,
averaging at a rating of 4.125/5 which means that the target level has been reached as it
is above the 3.5/5.
Finally, for the third test about the ”Quality of user experience”, the first, second, and fourth
biased user rated 5/5, while the third biased user rated 4/5, averaging at a rating of 4.75/5.
Again, the target level was reached by being above 4.3/5.
The biased users were not asked the fourth test case because of the previous exposure
to the system which might affect their confidence level after having tried to conduct a scan
multiple times.
Qualitative
Patient information flow
App flow
All the biased users navigated well through the Patient information flow. The first biased
user mentioned that ”it was super easy with the drop down options”, and the second biased
user stated that the flow ”was very logical and easy to run through.” The third biased user
said, ”it was really simple to navigate through” while the fourth added that it was ”user
friendly.”
Despite this, all biased users had challenges with the check boxes as they wanted to
check no when the patient answered no. This was not an option creating trouble with
the users. The first biased user stated ”for the check boxes, it would be more intuitive to
check yes when the patient says yes and no if they say no”. The second user explains
that this is due to ”us as nurses as we are very dutiful, so we want to make sure we have
been through everything and have not missed anything.” The fourth user agrees, and
adds ”it is pretty common in the health care sector to want to know that we have asked
the question even if the answer is no.”
Patient restrictions
The biased users responded positively when shown the patient related restrictions, men-
tioning that they would all skip the corresponding exercise(s). The second biased user
mentioned, that ”this is a good help for me as a nurse because why should the system
not help me. This is also something we are used to from the systems we work with, so
these reminders feel natural.”
When asked how they imagine the first screen in the Exercise flow would then look, the
first biased user says, that ”it would be very nice if the application then removes the ex-
ercise from the list of exercises” but the second biased user disagrees and emphasizes
the feeling of safety and security as they explain, ”I would prefer if [the exercise] was still
there but marked differently as you could be cheated by the system controlling everything
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
85
==PAGE:93==
for you. Also, I would like to explain to the patient why the procedure has changed for
them specifically to create some safety and security for the patient by knowing that I am
in control of the situation and it is not because I forgot to do something.” The third biased
user agrees as they imagine a case, where ”if the patient above 50 years old is running
marathons then I would still like to bypass the restriction and conduct the exercise. Even
though we have general guidelines at a hospital, how we use those guidelines really dif-
fers from department to department so most departments have their own instructions and
guidelines.”
Equipment flow
App flow
Overall, the biased users navigated adequately through the Equipment flow, and most
mentioned that it was easy to follow. However, three out of four biased users put on
equipment at different times then what was shown in the application.
The first biased user put on the cap right after measuring the patient’s head, connected
the ECG to the amplifier first before connecting the ECG srickers to the wire, and then
put the ECG on the patient’s wrists all before they were asked to do so by the application.
The user commented on their errors saying ”that is probably because I remembered what
to do from last time [the previous user test], so that was a little cheating” showing that the
errors were due to a clear bias.
The second biased user measured the patient’s head before clicking the ”Connect equip-
ment” button, turned on the amplifier then connected the cap to the amplifier, filled up
the gel in the syringe, and lastly connected the ECG to the amplifier again all before it
was shown in the application. The biased user stated, ”if I was doing this in real life, I
would probably not follow the guide exactly and just put on the equipment in the order it
felt intuitive. [...] I think it is difficult to have a completely perfect flow that all nurses follow
as we all do it a little differently.”
The fourth biased user put ECG on the patient’s wrists before they were asked in the
application but when noticing this, they stated ”okay I did it in the wrong order, but I think
it is not the biggest issue as long as I remember to do it.” This statement might also be
an example of bias as the dutifulness seen in nurses is replaced by the security of prior
knowledge from the user tests on the BrainCapture application (A.4), where the users
were shown that the order of the equipment was flexible to some degree.
Cap documentation
As mentioned, one step of putting on the equipment requires the user to measure the
patient’s head in order to choose the correct cap size for the patient. For this step, the
three color coordinated caps and their sizes are displayed. The second and third biased
users expressed a need for the possibility of choosing the selected cap by clicking on it.
The second biased user mentions documentation purposes as a reason saying, ”some-
86
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:94==
times, you have to go back if something happened in the results”. For the third biased
user, the justification lies in an increase in efficiency for dealing with reoccurring patients,
where they state ”if the patient comes back, I would not have to measure again, kind of
like when you go to the hairdresser where they already know how you want your hair cut.”
Animations
The animations which guided the users on how to equip the patients were popular amongst
all the biased users, as ”the animations were purposeful and made a lot of sense as it
made it easy to see what was going on” as stated by the first biased user.
The third biased user said ”the animations are really nice especially nowadays when we
search a lot on YouTube on how to do things - I think a lot of nurses especially learn by
doing and are visual learners.” One instance even showed that the animations acted as an
error prevention for the third biased user where they were asked to connect the amplifier
and app via Bluetooth, but the user wanted to connect the amplifier and the cap instead.
The user then quickly stopped themselves when looking at the animation and said, ”oh
okay I have to connect the device and the phone through Bluetooth”, and continued to do
so.
Also, the color choices made it ”less confusing on this version of the application because
there is not as much color [compared to the BrainCapture application], as here nothing
specific is taking away the overall focus, so I notice everything” as mentioned by the first
biased user.
Exercise flow
App flow
The fourth biased user talked about the flow mentioning, ”it is easily understandable gen-
erally and there is no place where I can start something before I should.” The rest of the
biased users shared consensus as they all navigated well through the flow.
However, some biased users showed confusion at the beginning of the first exercise when
seeing the screen displaying the ”Tap to start” button and the exercise information text.
About the ”Tap to start” button, the second biased user was confused whether they had
already started the exercise, and they explained ”I did not notice the ”Tap to start” button
at the top since the buttons have been at the bottom of the screen all the way throughout.”
They continued, ”it is not intuitive for me that I have to read some information text in the
middle of the screen but then look at the top of the screen to start, so I have to go back
and forth.” This challenge was also seen with the first biased user as they asked for
clarification on whether the intended information text was only for information before the
actual exercise started meaning that they might not have noticed the ”Tap to start” button
either.
In spite of all this, a majority of the biased users all used the exercise information text
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
87
==PAGE:95==
to explain what was going to happen to the patient showing that they used the text as a
tool throughout the examination proving its importance. Furthermore, the first biased user
stated, ”compared to the [BrainCapture application], it is nice that the colors of the text
are the same, so it creatives an equal focus, so I feel like I have to read everything, so
that is nice in this version.”
Annotations
In general, the biased users had different experiences with the annotation functionality.
Firstly, the first and third biased users found how to annotate challenging. For the an-
notation, the first biased user would like to write a comment after clicking an annotation
button. They explained, ”if I told the patient to keep their eyes open and they for some
reason did not, I would click the eyes open icon and write ”during this session, the patient
started blinking” so that it would be marked in the EEG to get the best result”. The third
biased user would like to annotate by clicking the ”annotation counter” which has the edit
functionality, and mentioned that despite seeing the annotation buttons with icons ”there is
an arrow, so I intuitively click it.” They therefore recommend that the ”annotation counter”
is moved below the buttons with icons ”so that I do not have as much focus on it. I am
just used to reading from the top to the bottom so I noticed that edit box before the other
buttons.”
Secondly, the dual purpose of annotating both exercises and deviations clearly caused
confusion. When finishing the ”blink continuously” part of the eyes exercise, both the first
and third biased users would ask the patient to talk. This pointed towards them under-
standing all the annotation buttons as being related to exercises, since ”the patient was
asked to do these things [eyes open, eyes closed, and blink continuously]. If the three
on top are exercises, then the next three at the bottom are also exercises, is how I un-
derstand it” the first biased user explained. The third biased user confirmed the claim by
saying, ”I feel like I have these tasks, open, close, and blink, and then I continue to talking
since that is the next. But it confuses me a bit cause the first three are tasks the patient
has to do while the next are artifacts, so not something the patient has to do.” The fourth
biased user finishes by stating, ”the only counter intuitive thing is having to annotate when
it is something I asked the patient to do and that it is placed the same place where you
have to annotate artifacts.”
This confusion was arguably due to bias, among other, as the first biased user explained,
”I remembered from [the user test on the BrainCapture application], that we only had to
annotate disturbances or artifacts, so that is why I am a little confused...” This is also
what the third and fourth biased users radiated where the latter states, ”last time [in the
BrainCapture application], I only had to annotate when the user did something they were
not asked to do.”
Nonetheless, successes were also present. Two of the biased users successfully under-
stood the annotation counter when adding an annotation, and three successfully removed
88
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:96==
an annotation. Lastly, three of four biased users correctly added an ”Other” annotation,
but two of them wanted to exit the sheet by clicking the ”X” without clicking the save check
mark at the bottom right corner of the sheet.
App feedback
Once an exercise has been completed, it changes to green in the first screen with the list
of exercises. Upon seeing this, the biased users felt positive where the second biased
user commented, ”I think the green is nice and means I can go to next” also stating that
they could use the change in color to ”update the patient about the progress.”
During the Exercise flow, the biased users were exposed to the three different alert mes-
sages where all of them responded efficiently by dealing with the alert first and some even
wanting to annotate after. For the orange ”Electrode T8 has lost connection!” alert, the
first biased user notes, ”it would be really nice if it would show me where the T8 electrode
is”, and the fourth biased user shares this wish by mentioning ”I would have liked to see
where the electrode is located so I could fix it and make sure everything is where it is
supposed to be.”
When the biased users had completed all the exercises, they saved the scan and saw
the confirmation. To this, the fourth biased user said ”it is always lovely with a big, green
check mark, so I know everything runs correctly.”
Review EEG flow
App flow
All the biased users successfully started the review of the scan by clicking the ”Review
scan” button on the Home screen.
Once the review was initiated, the biased users met the input fields with mixed feelings.
The first biased user stated, ”it is fine to write by myself but in terms of consistency of
results it would probably be better with some predefined options. If we as nurses would
have to write something ourselves, we would have to think about which kinds of words to
use when writing, some would not know what to write. That would make it easier for the
nurse when reviewing. But there should also be an option with the predefined options to
write something more if none of the predefined fit.” The second biased user agreed by
saying, ”it is very nice to not have to write by yourself for nurses as we are used to that
in the systems we work in that you click through options so that you do not have to write
and spell. So, I would like to have some predefined options and then still have the option
to add something if there was anything out of the ordinary. It is just 20 times easier than
having to formulate it by myself.”
The third and fourth biased users also raised different concerns in regards to the input
fields, where the former states ”this is something that you would have to be trained a bit
in so you know what to answer [...] It would be easier if there were some predefined
answers and maybe a note field at the bottom instead of writing everything down. That
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
89
==PAGE:97==
is more efficient.” The latter also mentions ”I live with two people who are dyslexic so
we have a lot of focus on what is difficult for dyslexic in the community. So, if there were
predefined options then they would not feel insecure about spelling or writing.”
Finally, all the biased users successfully navigated to edit an annotation, and upload the
scan when finished. The first biased user noted, ”now we have looked at it, and sent it
so everything should be good. And I can see it is in the cloud which is nice.” The second
biased user shares the excitement as they mentioned, ”it is nice that the scan is now in the
”Reviewed”, so I know that it has been sorted and I can clearly tell the difference between
the sorted and unsorted scans. It gives a good overview.” The fourth biased user happily
finishes, ”I love the big green check mark! It is so lovely.”
Overall application
Altogether, the biased users were positive on the improvements in this optimized version
of the application. The first biased user mentioned that the application ”is definitely more
user friendly than [the BrainCapture application] [...] I think I am more a fan of this one
compared to the one with colors [the BrainCapture application].” This, the fourth biased
user agreed with by saying, ”the app is very intuitive, and easy to use, and I have no
doubt where I should click in the app. Even though it is my first time using this version of
the app, I still find it very easy to use. [...] The app is a lot more user friendly than [the
BrainCapture application].”
The colors of the application also seem to have added on this opinion as the second
biased user stated, ”I liked the neutral blue colors better compared to the orange in [the
BrainCapture application] as there was not any noise in this one and other colors that
would disturb you when not necessary. So the app is nice and calm. Overall, nothing
disturbed me so in that sense the app did what it should.” Again, the third biased user
agreed by saying ”the colors of the app are better than [the BrainCapture application], as
I do not feel as alert from the orange color. [...] I think it is fine and comfortable for the
eyes to look at. [The BrainCapture application] was an alerting orange whereas this one
gives a more calm hospital vibe with the colors.”
In regards to the feedback presented to the user throughout, the third biased user men-
tioned, ”I really liked the feedback the app gave throughout [...] it is really nice to see that I
have done things correctly.” The fourth biased user echoes the statement by saying, ”the
app is good at making you aware if you are missing something or should be attentive on
something, and it guides me all the way.”
Improvements
Based on the test results, improvements have been made to both the application and the
user story map.
For the prototype, the check boxes in the Patient information flow which caused most
errors for the biased users have been replaced by ”Yes” and ”No” radio buttons which
90
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:98==
Figure 5.35: Updated radio buttons for ”Patient information flow”
allows the user to register the patient’s answers both when it is yes and no (see figure
5.35).
Figure 5.36: Added cap selection functionality
In the Equipment flow, the possibility to choose a cap size for documentation has been
implemented (see figure 5.36).
Multiple changes have been made in the Exercise flow.
First and most notably, the screen with the exercise information text has been replaced
by a modal component (see figure 5.37). Furthermore, the color, shape, and text of the
“Tap to start” button has been changed (see figure 5.37). Now, the button, which has the
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
91
==PAGE:99==
Figure 5.37: Updated improvements for the ”Exercise flow”
color and shape of a call-to-action button, has the text ”Start the timer”. These changes
have been made with the intention of counterattacking the misunderstandings the biased
users had in regards to most of them falsely believing that they had started the exercise.
Second, the placement of the timer has been moved from the top to the bottom of the
screen. Once the time is up, it changes to being a “Next” button (see figure 5.37).
Third, the annotation component was a high source of confusion. As mentioned, the
errors are to some extent a result of bias, but upon further research, it was discovered
that the similarity gestalt principle was violated. The principle is defined as, “elements
that share similar characteristics are perceived as more related than elements that do
not share those characteristics.” [28] The effect of this principle is seen as, the biased
users thought that all the annotation buttons had the same functionality. To respect this
principle and deal with the challenges of the users, the first row of annotation buttons which
were for exercises, and the second row which were for deviations have been changed so
that all the buttons are for annotating deviations (see figure 5.37). Here, eye and body
movement were added as predefined options as they were named as some of the most
common artifacts in the 3.2 User Needs section.
Moreover, the placement of the edit functionality was moved from the top of the annotation
component to the bottom to remove primary focus, and the check mark for saving an
”Other” annotation was removed so that it now saves automatically when clicking the ”X”
(see figure 5.37).
Fourth, some of the biased users wished to have a visualization of electrodes when getting
the ”Electrode T8 has lost connection!” alert message to more efficiently spot the electrode
so this was added as well (see figure 5.38).
92
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:100==
Figure 5.38: Added overview of EEG electrodes for alert message
Figure 5.39: Updated patient restrictions
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
93
==PAGE:101==
The fifth change was in regards to the patient restrictions, where the biased users wanted
the corresponding exercise to be marked differently rather than removed so that they
were reminded of the patient restrictions again. This was implemented by the exercises
being marked differently in the exercise list by getting a grey color. The user still has the
opportunity to bypass the restriction if they judge that the patient is capable to conduct
the exercise (see figure 5.39).
Figure 5.40: Added predefined options for ”Review EEG flow”
Lastly, the users had important arguments for replacing the input fields in the Review EEG
flow, and especially the fourth biased user shared their concern about dyslexia. Thus,
the input fields have been replaced by drop downs where the user can choose between
predefined options (see figure 5.40). The change also makes the application increases
accessibility to accommodate more users.
To interact with the V2 prototype, please refer to this link.
On the user story map, automatic annotation of all exercises have been added as system
tasks (A.7.2), so that this responsibility is removed from the user.
For the next sprint, the focus will be on the impact of the improvements and whether they
have solved the challenges, as well as new challenges if any occur. Other areas which
are consistently successful for the users will not be described further.
5.3.3
Third sprint
Test users
Four new test users were recruited for the testing round of the third sprint. The first test
user is a nurse with 12 years of experience where 10 of those years were at the Respira-
tory Medical Department at Hvidovre hospital, and the last two years were at the Ambu-
latory Surgery Department at the same hospital.
94
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:102==
The second test user works as a medical laboratory technologist at Roskilde universitet-
shospital, Region Sjælland. The user has five years of experience both in the laboratory
and with patients, and they therefore conduct very similar tasks to a nurse.
The third user is a social and health care assistant with two years of experience at the Neu-
rological department, and before that at the Acute Rehabilitation department at Roskilde
universitetshospital, Region Sjælland. Apart from giving injections to patients, the social
and health care assistant has the same tasks at the hospital as a nurse.
Lastly, the fourth user is a nurse and clinical instructor with 10 years of experience at the
Ambulatory Surgery Department at Hvidovre hospital.
Test results
Quantitative
For the third sprint, the four users made three errors on average where the first and second
users both had two errors each, and the third and fourth users had four errors each. With
the new baseline level from the last sprint at five errors, the target level of the first test
case about ”Low error rates” is successful.
For the second test case, about ”User attraction to product”, the first and third users rated
5/5, while the second and fourth users rated 4.5/5 averaging at 4.75/5. Again, with the
new baseline level from the last sprint at 4.125/5, the target level of the second test case
is successful.
For the third test about the ”Quality of user experience”, the first and second users rated
5/5, the third user rated 4/5, and the fourth user rated 4.5/5 averaging at a rating of 4.625/5.
The third test case is therefore not successful with the new baseline level from the last
sprint at 4.75/5.
When asked how confident the user would be to conduct a scan tomorrow, the average
rating was 4.5/5. All users rated 5/5 except for the second who rated 3/5. Here, no
new baseline level is set since the users of the last sprint were not asked this question.
Therefore, the original target level set at 4.5/5 in table 5.3 has been reached.
Qualitative
Patient information flow
App flow
The users described the flow as easy to use and straight forward, and the first user ex-
plained ”it is easy to use since it just has a question, something to click and something to
fill out, so it was very straight forward.”
The improvements therefore seemed to work well as none of the users had any difficulties
while navigating through the Patient information flow.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
95
==PAGE:103==
Lastly, the third user praised the design of the flow and said ”it is very manageable, nice,
and it does not have too much text which could create confusion. It is also nice that it
shows that there are four pages of questions because to me the predictability creates a
good overview.”
Patient restrictions
The patient restrictions and the greyed out corresponding exercises were presented to
the users. While all the users responded positively, three out of four users mentioned the
need for extra confirmation after clicking a greyed out exercise.
The first user stated, ”I would still like to be able to click it in case I, as a nurse, determine
that it is safe for the patient. When that is said, I would imagine that an extra pop up would
be there to confirm just in case.” Here, the third user agreed by saying, ”in some cases
where I see that it is still possible, there should be an extra confirmation before I would
start the exercise so it is sure that it is not a mistake.” Lastly, the fourth user reasoned
patient safety by stating, ”if I still want to do the exercise, I would want another confirmation
just to be sure before doing it because that is how it is in our systems at work. That would
be a good idea in terms of patient safety.”
Equipment flow
Cap documentation
Two out of four users successfully clicked to select the chosen cap in the application. One
of the users who did not do it was the fourth, as ”intuitively, I would think it is enough to just
take the cap but when I think about it, it makes sense for the next person who is reading
the scan to know which cap has been used. I think the reason why I did not find it intuitive
is because I was busy with the equipment.”
App flow
Generally, all the users navigated well through the Equipment flow. The first user stated,
”it was also very straight forward and a very pedagogical guidance with both text and
animations of what and how to do it. [...] The animations were nice since it showed me
where to plug in the wires, etc. so it was a lot faster for me than reading and I did not
have to think hard. It kept showing me the way to go.” The second user agreed as they
found that ”it made really good sense. I had zero prerequisites and I could do it, so I think
it made really good sense.”
However, the third and fourth user had minor challenges throughout the flow. The third
user wanted to put the cap on the patient’s head before clicking the ”Connect equipment”
button, and to connect the cap to the amplifier before they were asked in the application.
The user commented, ”generally, I thought it was easy but there were times when I wanted
to do things in a different order or times when I was too fast.”
The fourth user wanted to connect the cap to the amplifier before it was asked in the
96
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:104==
application, and noted, ”I am very focused on the practical aspect of the equipment and
want to finish connecting the cap before connecting the app with Bluetooth. [...] it would
have made more sense to me to finish the steps about the cap before connecting the
app.”
Exercises flow
Information text
The exercise information text still seemed to be a challenge to some of the users. Upon
seeing the information text, the first user seemed to think, they were already doing the
exercise as they explained ”the first screen had a lot of text compared to the rest of the
app and I just needed some time to think how I should act in the screen. I already thought
I was doing the exercise, and I did not notice that it was information text.” The fourth user
also did not seem to understand the information text as they stated, ”I am not sure if I
should say this information to the patient or just check if they do it or something else.”
Despite this, the exercise information text did seem to make sense to both the second
and third users, as they both used the text to explain to the patient how the exercise was
going to be conducted.
In the information text, it is written that the user has to remember to annotate. To this the
first and third user both agreed that they would have liked ”if it said in the exercise text
what I have to annotate since it is only deviations I have to annotate” as stated by the first
user. The third user continued, ”it would have been nice if it said somewhere in the app
that I should only annotate when the patient does something they should not do. I think
that would help me.”
All in all, while clear challenges will need to be accommodated, the updated changes have
made improvements for the user’s understanding and navigation of the Exercise flow.
Annotations
Overall, all the users successfully understood the annotation component, and annotated
when the patient deviated from the exercises meaning that the changes had improved the
experience. The second user did comment, ”I would have a piece of paper next to me
where I would write it down. I do not see where I could do it in the app.”
But when told that it is a possibility in the application, and where they imagine it would
be possible to annotate, the user pointed to the annotation component. From there they
then successfully annotated deviations using the annotation buttons. Going forward, the
user had adapted to the annotation component, finishing with ”the annotation part made
really good sense.”
However, a challenge was apparent when the users were asked to annotate a cough.
While half of the users successfully clicked the ”Other” annotation button to annotate the
cough, the other half wanted to click the ”Body movement” annotation button. The first
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
97
==PAGE:105==
user explained, ”coughing creates body movement” which the second user also stated.
When asked to keep looking, the attention of the first user directed towards the ”Other”
annotation button, and the user noted, ”actually this makes more sense. I would probably
have chosen this one in the end since coughing was not an option, so I would out of
curiosity click ”Other” to see what other options there were.”
Another challenge half of the users were met with was when they were asked to edit an
annotation. Here, the first and second users successfully navigated to edit an annotation,
and the latter commented, that to them it was ”self explanatory that I have to click here to
edit an annotation since you have the total amount of annotations.” For the third and fourth
users, it was not as self explanatory. The third user tried to click the ”Other” annotation
button while explaining ”there is not really anything else that points towards an edit option.”
The fourth user, firstly tried to click the ”Skip” button and then tried to click the same
annotation again hoping to remove an annotation. The user commented, ”my intuition
was to kind of go one back or skip something I just did. Or maybe I could click on the
same annotation and it would reverse it.”
Alert messages
The added visualization of electrodes to the ”Electrode lost connection” alert was praised
by all the users. The first user noted, ”it was nice that it shows me which electrode it is
and where, since I am not familiar with it.” The fourth user also added, ”the illustration
made it easy to understand what to do” meaning that the improvement added value for
the users.
Review EEG flow
App flow
The first user stated that the flow was ”very intuitive and straight forward.” The user con-
tinued, ”I think it makes sense to have to click through the options. We have gotten used
to Sundhedsplatformen where we also have to click through a lot of things so I am used
to this kind of interaction. Also, I think it is also really nice that there is an option to further
write something if needed” where the second user added, ”it was the same as when we
do journals so it was easy.” This shows that the added dropdown menus with predefined
options clearly had a positive impact on the user’s navigation experience.
Still, the third and fourth users felt, that it was not clear that they could choose multiple
options on the first review question. The third user commented, ”it was not clear that I
could choose more, but I started suspecting it when the drop down did not go away after
clicking on one option. It could be more clear that it is possible to choose more options.”
The fourth user added, ”usually it would say if I can choose multiple options.”
A majority of the users easily navigated to edit an annotation, where the fourth user stated,
”it was intuitive because the pen means to edit.”
The third user, however, had misunderstood, and stated, ”I think the main issue is that it
98
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:106==
is in English, because if it was in Danish I would have understood it quicker.”
Furthermore, the second user expressed that they would like for it to be possible to delete
an annotation as well, by explaining ”I would like to also be able to delete an annotation
just in case I had clicked something in a rush and then forgotten to delete it. So it would
be nice before uploading the scan, to have the ability of going through the annotations
and make sure everything makes sense.”
Once the users had finished the review, they all successfully uploaded the scan to the
cloud. The first user then commented, ”it is nice for me to see how many are left to
do and how many have been done but it would have been nice if there was a bigger
difference between to do and done so it is even easier to spot.” This, the fourth user
agreed with as they had not noticed that the scan had been moved to ”Reviewed”. The
user explained, ”I did not notice a difference, but when I read it I do see that there are two
different categories. For me it would have been easier to distinguish if they had different
colors.”
Overall application
Users had positive comments about the overall application. The first and second user
mentioned that the application made sense for them, the latter stating ”it makes sense. I
think anyone, even some who are not in the field, would be able to do it with the proper
training. Everything is shown in the app, so I think even my little sister could do it.”
The application also brought a feeling of safety and security where the first user explained
that ”the app made me feel safe and I did not feel like I could make a fatal error.” The
second user also mentioned that ”the app guided me all the way through and gave me all
prerequisites.” The third user continued, ”it is very simple and there is guidance all the
way throughout and to me that is the most important because it creates safety for me”
while the fourth user added ”the app guided me well and I felt very safe.”
Finally, the design of the application was especially praised as the first user mentioned ”the
design is user friendly, and the colors are calm and neutral.” The second user explained,
”the graphical design and explanations are good.” The third user agreed, by saying that
they ”really liked the animations in the app since many of us understand better visually.”
Finally, the fourth user mentioned, that ”the app is very aesthetic and calming to look at.”
Improvements
The test results presented the need for improvements. Again, these improvements have
been implemented to the prototype and the user story map.
During the Equipment flow, some users put on parts of the equipment in a different order
than what was shown in the prototype. To accommodate this need, the order of putting
the equipment on is changed so that “Connect the amplifier and the cap by connecting
the wire” comes before “Connect the amplifier and app via Bluetooth by choosing the
amplifier” (see figure 5.41). With this new order, the user can ”complete” the steps of
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX

==PAGE:107==
Figure 5.41: Updated ”Equipment flow”
the cap before continuing to deal with the amplifier and app. This was also added as a
corresponding user task in the user story map (A.7.3.)
For the Exercise flow, again multiple improvements have been made.
Figure 5.42: Change in exercise information text and the annotation component
First, the exercise information text has been reformulated to make it more clear that it
is information text, also the duration of each exercise has been included in the text (see
figure 5.42). Furthermore, it has been specified what the user has to annotate.
Second, the “edit annotations” icon on the annotation component has been changed from
a chevron icon to a pencil icon which is inspired by the ease of use that users felt when
100
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:108==
seeing the same pencil icon during the Review EEG flow (see figure 5.42). Moreover, it
adds consistency to have the same icon for editing functionality.
Figure 5.43: Added confirmation pop up for patient restrictions
The third improvement is in regards to the patient restrictions where users wanted to see
a final confirmation if they wanted to bypass the restrictions and still conduct the exercise.
Therefore, a pop up has been added, which states, ”The patient is not eligible to do this
exercise. Are you sure you want to continue?” Here, the user can then click the ”Cancel”
button if they regret or the ”Continue” button if they still want to conduct the exercise (see
figure 5.43).
Figure 5.44: Updated improvements in the ”Review EEG flow”

blue secondary color was replaced by the positive green color. This was done to make
the difference between the two sections, ”To review” and ”Reviewed”, more distinguished
(see figure 5.44).

5.3.4
Fourth sprint
Test users
Again, four new test users were recruited for the testing round of the fourth sprint. The
first test user is a clinical nurse specialist at the Nephrology Department at Roskilde uni-
versitetshospital with eight years of experience in total.
The second user is a nurse with 14 years of experience at the Cardiology Department at
the same hospital.
The third user is an assisting head nurse with seven years of experience at the Respiratory
Medical Department at Hvidovre hospital.
Finally, the fourth user is a nurse with 10 years of experience as a home care nurse, and
one year of experience at the Respiratory Medical Department at Hvidovre hospital.
Test results
Quantitative
For the fourth sprint, the four users made a little over two (2.25) errors on average where
the first and fourth users both had three errors each, the second user had two errors, and
third user had one error. Based on the new baseline level of three errors from the last
sprint, the target level of the test case about ”Low error rates” is successful.
For the second test case, about ”User attraction to product”, the first and fourth users
rated 4/5, while the second and third users rated 5/5 averaging at 4.5/5. With the new
baseline level of the last sprint being 4.75/5, the target level has not been reached.
For the third test about the ”Quality of user experience”, all users rated 4.5/5 except for
the first who rated 4/5 averaging at 4.375/5. Therefore, with a new baseline level from the
previous sprint at 4.625/5, the target level of the test case has not been reached.
When asked how confident the user would be to conduct a scan tomorrow, the average
102
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:110==
rating was 4.875/5. All users rated 5/5 except for the first user who rated 4.5/5. Therefore,
with the new baseline level at 4.5/5 from the last sprint, the target level of the test case
has been reached.
Qualitative
Patient information flow
About the pop up confirmation triggered by the user clicking on a greyed out exercise, due
to the patient restrictions, the third user commented, ”it makes sense to get a warning. It
makes sense that I should still be allowed to do the exercise if I believe that the patient is
healthy enough. But it is smart that it shows an extra confirmation since it is easy to mis-
takenly click the exercise if you are in a hurry.” The fourth user added that for them it also
”makes sense to get an extra confirmation if it is decided to do the exercise otherwise.”
Equipment flow
All four users navigated easily through the Equipment flow and followed the order shown
in the application showing the positive impact of the implemented improvements. The third
user mentioned that for them the flow ”was very intuitive”, and the second user stated, ”it
was very easy because it both says in text what to do and it also has animations which
guided very well so it was very straight forward.”
However, new challenges arose for the first and second users. When reaching the ”Con-
nect the ECG to the wires” screen, the first user confusedly stated, ”I am unsure what to
do now.” The same screen also confused the second user as upon reaching this screen,
the user took the wires but was unsure what to do next, and said, ”I am unsure what I
should connect them to.” The user continued, ”I believe we call it something else than
wires when we work with ECG.”
Exercises flow
Information text
All the users found the amount of text for the exercise information text to be too excessive.
Upon seeing the text, the first user was unsure whether the exercise had started. The user
noted, ”I thought I was already doing the exercise and had to ask the patient to do it [...]
I actually do not think I personally need this text if it is only meant as an introduction for
the exercise. I think it is more important that it is straight forward without so much text.”
Despite successfully understanding the exercise information text as an introduction to
the exercise, and using it to guide the patient, the second user echoed the statement by
saying, ”the first exercise screen might have a little too much text but otherwise it was
easy.”
Like the first, the third user also thought they had already started the exercise as they
started directing the patient. The user then commented, ”oh wait, it says start the timer. I
should have read the whole thing. Maybe there is a little too much text.”
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX

It seems, that the reformulation of the exercise information text has created more chal-

Finally, the fourth user, who successfully understood the text as an introduction to the
exercise, mentioned ”it confused me a bit in the beginning when it told me what I had to
do but it did not tell me where to annotate, it only told me to annotate.”
Annotations
The majority of the users successfully understood the annotation component and anno-
tated deviations.
When the second user was asked to annotate, the user went to click where it is meant
to edit an annotation, and explained, ”I would click here because it has the word anno-
tations and on the previous page it said to annotate, also it has a pencil so I thought I
was supposed to write something.” When it is revealed where it should be annotated, the
user said, ”okay, that makes sense. Then it is even easier than having to write. Often, we
have to do a lot of documentation so it is very nice that it is easy to just click on instead of
writing.” When asked to edit an annotation the user commented ”now it is the pencil and
that makes sense.” The rest of the users all successfully edited an annotation as well,
the fourth user commenting that they ”look for the pencil to edit.”
Thus, the change of icon, from chevron to pencil, has proved its impact by creating great
value for the user’s understanding.
Review EEG flow
All users navigated well through the Review EEG flow, where the third and fourth users
successfully understood that it was possible to choose multiple options for the first ques-
tion. For the first and second users, however, this was not clear. The latter stated ”it was
not clear since on all the other ones it was only possible to click once.”
Despite this, the first user excitedly mentioned, ”I think it is really nice that it is not just a
clicking system but it also includes some health care considerations from the nurse, I like
that as it gives a good picture of the process and situation [...] it requires us nurses to
think and use our knowledge and dive deeper.” The second user agreed stating, ”I think
it is very nice to be able to evaluate it like this. It makes really good sense to reflect on
the scan, evaluate it and upload it”.
To test the added deletion functionality in the annotations, the users were asked to delete
the second annotation which they all easily accomplished. About this functionality, the
first user stated, ”I think it is nice that there is the option to edit and delete annotations
later.” Thus, the added improvement has successfully accomplished its purpose.
Once the users had uploaded the scan, they all reacted positively, where the fourth user
expressed ”it makes me happy cause then I know that I have done it right. It is just missing
104
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:112==
a *pling* sound.” The second user commented ”it is smart that it is divided in which scans

Overall application
The users all mentioned that they found the application to be straight forward and good
at guiding them through all the steps. The first user stated, ”I do not think it was hard, it is

be able to. There are not too many questions including possible options for each question,
also the images to guide. [...] The app takes it one step at a time, so for someone who
knows nothing about it, it is super nice. It does not seem too hard to do.”
Furthermore, the third and fourth user praised the design of the application, where the
third mentioned, ”the colors of the app are nice and neutral” and the fourth stated, ”the
design is easy on the eyes.”
Improvements
The users of the testing round in the fourth sprint performed well throughout the whole
application leaving a need for only minor improvements.
Figure 5.45: Change in wording for the information text for the ECG

Second, the exercise information text in the Exercise flow created the most disturbance for

the first time they do so (see figure 5.46). Once, the user has read the ”Tip” and click on
the ”Continue” button, the user is presented with the aforementioned exercise information

Test users
For the final test round, four new test users were recruited all working at the Respiratory

The first test user is a newly educated nurse with five months of experience.
The second test user is a social and health care assistant with seven years of experience.

Once again, the social and health care assistant has the same tasks as a nurse except

The third user is a nurse with two and a half years of experience, and the fourth user is a

Test results
Quantitative
For the final sprint, the four users had one and half errors on average. The first user had
two errors, the second user had four errors, and the third and fourth users had zero errors
each. With a new baseline level from the previous sprint of a little over two errors, the
target level of the test case about ”Low error rates” is successful.
For the second test case, about ”User attraction to product”, all users rated 5/5 except for
the first who rated 4/5 which averages to 4.75/5, and with the new baseline level of the
previous sprint being 4.5/5, the target level is reached.
For the third test about the ”Quality of user experience”, once again all users rated 5/5
except for the first who rated 4/5 averaging at 4.75/5. Thus, with a new baseline level
from the previous sprint at 4.375/5, the target level is reached.
Lastly, for the fourth test case all users rated 5/5 which is an average of 5 meaning that
the target level of the test case is successful.
Qualitative
Patient information flow
All the users navigated easily through the Patient information flow, and the third user
commented, ”It was very easy. It is the standard patient information that I would ask
nearly no matter what I have to do with the patient.”
The first user raised a concern for patient security by questioning, ”would the app tell me
if I wrote a wrong digit in the personal identification? I would hope that it would to increase
the patient security and to decrease unintentional incidents.”
Equipment flow
Three out of four test users easily navigated through the Equipment flow by following the
instructions in the application, and the fourth user stated that the flow was ”very intuitive
as it tells you everything you need to do.”
The second user, however, wanted to put on the cap before measuring the patient’s head,
and did not turn on the amplifier. On this, they commented, ”I am maybe a bit too fast and
should probably read what it says.”
Overall, the change in wording for connecting the ECG seems to have made a general
improvement.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
107
==PAGE:115==
While navigating through the flow, the third user asked ”how much gel should I use?”.
The first user also questioned this and reasoned, ”it would be nice if it told me how many
milliliter of gel I need to fill in the syringe. We are very conscious of giving the right dose.”
Exercises flow
The users started the first exercise, and half of the users successfully told the patient to
keep their eyes closed and open their jaw slightly upon seeing the ”Tip” pop up.
Then all the users saw and used the exercise information text to introduce the patient to
the exercise. The third user commented, ”I also liked that it showed me where to annotate
in the exercise information, and I think that once I have done the scan many times, it will
be imprinted. So it is a nice hidden nudge towards increasing the security of the nurse
annotating correctly.” Thus, the addition of the image was a clear improvement for the
users.
Overall application
The users reacted well to the application as a whole and responded positively throughout.
Especially the design of the user interface was complemented, the third user stating ”the
design of the app is streamlined with the same design all throughout so it does not confuse
you with something new [...] The app has the same colors and images throughout so it is
very easy to look at the app visually. It was a very calm looking app so I did not have to
spend a lot of energy on focusing my eyes.”
The fourth user also mentioned, ”the design is lovely and everything is nice and big. It
is pretty, there are green check marks so it is superb. [...] The design is straight forward
without too much randomness.”
Also the usability of the application was praised. The second user found that the applica-
tion ”is very smart, understandable, useful, easy to use, and guides me well.”
The third user continued jokingly, ”the app was very user friendly. I am not very tech savvy
so if I can do it then everyone can. [...] I am one of those who throws my phone at the
wall when something is too confusing so giant koodous.”
The fourth user finished by stating, ”it was super intuitive. It makes super good sense and
we are used to working with information like this, so it is very intuitive for a nurse.”
Final comments
During the tests, the second user failed to follow the order of equipment as instructed by
the application.
Furthermore, it was apparent that the users were missing details about the amount of gel
to use. In their practice, this is very important to know, as stated by the users, but when
filling up the cap with gel, the amount of gel is flexible to some extent as the main goal is
to use the gel until the electrodes are connected and they turn green. That said, for an in-
108
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:116==
crease of the overall user experience, it would be an option to include an average amount
of gel to use, despite it not having a direct impact on the end result of the examination.
Also, it was seen that only half of the users followed the ”Tip” pop up. Since quality control
plays an important role in conducting a quality scan, an improvement is needed through
further development. However, when looking through all the previous sprints, none of
the users had specifically asked their patient to close their eyes or open their jaw slightly.
Thus, the ”Tip” pop up effectively made more users instruct their patient correctly to better
the overall quality.
Moreover, the added image of the annotation component to the exercise information
modal proved to be fruitful as seen through the user’s response.
5.4
Sub-conclusion
Through the MoSCoW Prioritization, the functionalities were filtered to asses which of
them were to be included in the optimized application at an MVP level. Here, inputting
patient information including routine questions, patient restrictions for exercises, prepar-
ing equipment including cap, amplifier, ECG, syringe, gel, and measuring tape, conduct-
ing exercises (eyes, hyperventilation, and photic stimulation) with guidance, annotation
contributing to quality control, reviewing of the scan covering a preliminary evaluation,
uploading examination to the cloud, and flexible system respecting free navigation were
all functionalities to be included.
During the design implementation, the user interface and user experience design of the
initial version of the prototype were established. Especially the exercise information text
proved to be challenging as multiple users would misunderstand. However, through four
rounds of testing, the application was improved iteratively based on the user’s test re-
sults, and for the final sprint, it was seen that all test cases for the quantitative tests were
successful, and for the qualitative tests, only minor improvements were discovered.
Thus, the final version of the application (V4) is an eligible offer of how an optimized
version of the BrainCapture application could look in terms of improved user interface
and user experience design.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
109
==PAGE:117==
6
Discussion
Throughout the thesis, the direct and indirect users and their needs have been discovered,
the BrainCapture system including their prototype and application, has been adjudged
and risks have been assessed. Finally, an optimized MVP prototype has been developed
iteratively through a data-driven process.
The 6 Discussion chapter will discuss different aspects of the thesis chapters including
the importance of failing fast, initial design decisions, test users biases, and more. Con-
clusively, it will discuss whether the optimized prototype is better than BrainCapture’s.
6.1
Research
The users and their user needs were described through expert interviews and EEG obser-
vations which created a fundamental basis for both the content of the 4 Analysis chapter
and the 5 Design Implementation & Testing chapter.
It is however important to consider that the data was collected in Denmark at Danish hos-
pitals with Danish neurophysiology assistants, medical laboratory technologists, doctors,
and patients. The intended market is LMICs and especially Kenya has been a target.
For these countries, other needs and challenges might be present as a result of different
resources, training, or other environmental and cultural factors. Thus, the collected data
is not fully representative of the intended user’s needs but it lays down the foundational
groundwork.
The same can be said for the recruited test users who were all also Danish.
6.2
Analysis
The BrainCapture prototype was held against metrics through a variety of methods to
adjudge whether the prototype satisfied the individual sections of the User Experience
Honeycomb. Each tool was sensibly chosen for a thorough assessment, but the con-
clusion of whether each section was satisfied is to some degree depending on a biased
threshold.
That being said, the threshold set to conclude the results was focused on a comprehensive
assessment based on both relevant knowledge and experience within the field and is
therefore still valid and valuable.
Furthermore, a challenge which showed itself mostly at the end of the thesis was working
with the BrainCapture prototype versus the application. The prototype was the static end
goal for the application, so at the beginning of the thesis, it was decided to focus on the
prototype. At the same time, the company conducted user tests on the application, and
the results were valuable as they gave an overview of usability challenges. Therefore, it
was desired to include the results to use as a tool to highlight points and findings.
110
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:118==
However, the company moves relatively fast on implementing new features to their ap-
plication. Joggling between the two proved to be a challenge as inconsistency between
them were at times difficult to describe. A solution could have been to conduct usability
tests on the BrainCapture prototype and use those results in the analysis. Thus, the rapid
implementation of the application would be an independent factor which could run par-
allel with the thesis. Once the thesis had reached its end, the company would have the
opportunity to sit with both user test results on their desired end state and an optimized
data-driven MVP prototype whereafter they could decide on how to go forth.
An exmample of an inconsistent feature between the prototype and application was the
exercise timer. In the user tests on the BrainCapture application, users found it stressful
that the exercise timer started before they felt ready. A feature for the user to start the
timer was clearly missing from the application. However, when looking at the BrainCap-
ture prototype, this feature is available. Thus, at some point during the implementation of
the application, this feature was overlooked and when the application was finally tested,
it created inconveniences for the users which inevitably worsened the overall user expe-
rience.
It is an interesting phenomenon seen in companies, be it small or large, that the link be-
tween the user experience and development team at times goes missing, creating silos
between the two. It is clear that preliminary usability tests on the prototype could have
been beneficial, and testing on the prototype before starting development on the applica-
tion could have counterattacked the challenge.
Since multiple users met this challenge in the user tests, it is highly likely that the chal-
lenge would have been discovered in preliminary tests, also since it is a violation of basic
usability guidelines as discussed. Thus, the importance of failing fast through early testing
is eminent.
Another aspect in regards to testing is difference in test setup. As mentioned, the Brain-
Capture application was tested by the usability expert, where the focus was also highly
on the cap management. Furthermore, the users were given an introduction to epilepsy
and EEG. Also, the users had to deal with human patients.
This was not the case for the usability testing for the optimized application. Here, the
tests was centered only around the user’s interaction with the user interface and were
conducted through simulations. Therefore, the test users did not have to worry about the
cap being placed correctly, the filling of the cap, or similar concerns.
Thus, the users for the BrainCapture user tests were challenged to a greater extent as
they had more to undergo. It is therefore conceivable, that the additional challenges
had a possible negative impact on the overall experience of the BrainCapture application
which the test users in the usability testing of the optimized application may not have
experienced.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
111
==PAGE:119==
6.3
Design Implementation & Testing
Deciding on the initial design of the user interface of the optimized application was an
arduous task which required many considerations and choices before finally reaching a
satisfactory state. Despite all the deliberation and contemplation, some aspects were still
overlooked. Most notably the color hierarchy.
The usage of colors and its potential impact on users was discussed during the analysis
as the colors of the BrainCapture application were examined. Despite this knowledge,
when it came to designing the optimized version it was not considered, showing that
in practice, human mistakes happen. Also, it furthermore underlines that sparring and
reviewing early stages of solutions with individuals of the same profession can be just as
important as getting feedback from users.
In regards to feedback from the users, the test user’s error rates were measured during
the quantitative testing, and it was clear that for each testing round the average error
rate decreased which is shown in figure 6.1. Thus, it is seen that working in an iterative
build-measure-learn cycle proved to have a positive effect on the error rates.
Figure 6.1: Average user errors for each test round
When looking more into the data, it was interesting to see that the social and health care
assistants typically had more errors than both the nurses and medical laboratory technol-
ogists.
In total, two social and health care assistants participated in the usability testing of the
optimized application, namely the third user in testing round two for sprint three, and the
112
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:120==
second user in testing round four for the final sprint. Both of them had four errors each
which were either the highest or amongst the highest amounts for each respective testing
round.
The amount of users included in the usability testing with this role is not enough to be
fully representative, but one main finding was that while the different roles (nurse, medical
laboratory technologists, and social and health care assistants) might conduct very similar
tasks at the hospital, they seem to have different behavioral patterns. During the testing
rounds, nurses and medical laboratory technologists were notably more calm, read the
information text, and followed the application dutifully, however they were at times careful
and wanted approval before navigating further. For the social and health care assistants,
the case was reversed. They seemed eager and hands-on resulting in them at times
making hasty errors. The different training from their respective educational backgrounds
reflects in how they approach their work creating an influential bias.
Thus, it is important to be aware of these biases when recruiting test users as the be-
havioural patterns of the users inevitably will affect the test results.
6.4
The final result
Throughout the thesis, it was discovered that the BrainCapture solution, be it the appli-
cation or the prototype, has both strengths and weaknesses. The user’s overall opinions
in the user tests were relatively positive but usability challenges were clear. Through the
user experience review in the 4 Analysis chapter, it was also concluded that the solution
was challenged.
The optimized application is an MVP which focused on solving some of the main weak-
nesses, especially on two of the main challenges in regards to user needs and accessi-
bility.
One key user need recurrently mentioned was the need for having a flexible system. As
mentioned, the linear flow, particularly for the exercises, of the BrainCapture application
constraints the user’s ability to flexibly move in the flow. Here, the optimized application’s
list of exercises both guides and respects the user’s choices.
For accessibility, especially the colors of the BrainCapture prototype, violated contrast
ratio guidelines which could cause different inconviences and legal risks as mentioned in
the 4.2 SWOT section. In the optimized application, the colors all satisfy the contrast ratio
guidelines. Furthermore, the annotation component of the optimized application provides
lower physical effort than the BrainCapture solution because of its construction which
makes it possible to annotate with a lower amount of clicks.
Thus, if BrainCapture were to adopt the optimized prototype, they would be able to ac-
commodate more users with possible disabilities, and would be free from worrying about
change in national legislation.
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
113
==PAGE:121==
Lastly, the optimized application is based on a data-driven approach focusing on quantita-
tive and qualitative data collection. Having a product or service which has been iteratively
optimizied, results in an user-centric output which was created for the user by the user.
6.4.1
Future work
While the final result presented has reached a satisfactory level, further refinement through
additional sprints would bring more value.
While the application has been through five sprints with feedback sessions and testing
rounds, feedback on the current state from expert roles could be highly relevant to asses
whether the application covered all the requirements they would see fit.
As mentioned, the inteded market is LMICs, thus conducting additional usability tests on
the African nurses could lead to new insights and epiphanies which could be used to
optimize the application all the more.
Furthermore, the optimized application is as mentioned at an MVP state, and therefore
only covers the main analyzed challenges and weaknesses discovered in the BrainCap-
ture application. In future iterations, it would be beneficial to look into other uncovered
risks. Thereafter, it would also be profitable to look at how to expand the MVP to include
additional features. Here, the could have and will not have functionalities mentioned in the
5.1 MoSCoW Prioritization section would be intuitive targets for further implementation
ideas, as well as the tasks in slice two and three in the user story map (A.7.3).
114
Data-driven optimization of UI & UX
==PAGE:122==
7
Conclusion
In this thesis, the direct and indirect users of a typical epilepsy diagnosis flow were pre-
sented including the patient, personal doctor, neurophysiology assistant, neurophysiolo-
gist, and neurologist. However, it was stated that these roles were not available in LMICs
meaning that other roles such as nurses would have to fill the role of the neurophysiology
assistant. Thus, other user needs would be present. These needs were then discov-
ered through expert interviews and EEG observations. The user needs covered themes
such as EEG scan including patient information, equipment and exercises, flexible sys-
tem, quality control including artifacts, review of EEG, and synchronized video, challenges
including patient behavior and seizure handling, and lastly other illnesses.
These user needs, alongside previous results from user tests, a focus group, and other
methodologies were used to assess different aspects of the BrainCapture solution with a
focus on user interface and user experience. Here, it was concluded that the solution had
over weighing weaknesses, and risks which ultimately meant that the development of an
optimized version at an MVP state could be initiated focusing on data driven approaches.
Finally, the new version was tested went through five sprints of iterations including four
rounds of testing with relevant test users. Each iteration followed the build-measure-learn
cycle, and improvements were discovered and implemented throughout. At the end of
the final sprint, a final version of the optimized MVP was reached. The results of the
usability tests showed that the optimized version is better in terms of usability, and the
user’s opinions on the user interface and overall user experience.
Thus, the user interface and user experience of the BrainCapture application was ad-
judged, and an optimal version in terms of user interface and user experience using data-
driven approaches was delivered.
